\begin{example}[Moments of Normal Sufficient
Statistics]\protect\hypertarget{exm-normal-sufficient-moments}{}\label{exm-normal-sufficient-moments}

Consider \(X_i \sim N(\mu, \sigma^2)\).

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Log-Likelihood Form} The standard log-likelihood is: \[
   \ell(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{x}) = \frac{\mu}{\sigma^2} \sum x_i - \frac{1}{2\sigma^2} \sum x_i^2 - \left[ \frac{n\mu^2}{2\sigma^2} + \frac{n}{2} \log(2\pi\sigma^2) \right]
   \]

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Natural Parameters:
    \(\eta_1 = \frac{\mu}{\sigma^2}, \quad \eta_2 = -\frac{1}{2\sigma^2}\).
  \item
    Log-Partition Function (in terms of \(\boldsymbol{\eta}\)): Using
    \(\sigma^2 = -\frac{1}{2\eta_2}\) and
    \(\mu = -\frac{\eta_1}{2\eta_2}\): \[
    A(\boldsymbol{\eta}) = -\frac{n \eta_1^2}{4 \eta_2} - \frac{n}{2} \log(-2\eta_2) + \frac{n}{2}\log(2\pi)
    \]
  \end{itemize}

  \textbf{Canonical Log-Likelihood} \(\ell(\boldsymbol{\eta})\): \[
   \ell(\boldsymbol{\eta}; \mathbf{x}) = \eta_1 \left(\sum x_i\right) + \eta_2 \left(\sum x_i^2\right) - \left[ -\frac{n \eta_1^2}{4 \eta_2} - \frac{n}{2} \log(-2\eta_2) \right]
   \]
\item
  \textbf{First Moments (Means)} \[
   E[T_1] = E\left[\sum X_i\right] = \frac{\partial A}{\partial \eta_1} = -\frac{2n\eta_1}{4\eta_2} = -\frac{n\eta_1}{2\eta_2} = n\mu
   \] \[
   E[T_2] = E\left[\sum X_i^2\right] = \frac{\partial A}{\partial \eta_2} = \frac{n\eta_1^2}{4\eta_2^2} - \frac{n}{2(-2\eta_2)}(-2) = \frac{n\eta_1^2}{4\eta_2^2} - \frac{n}{2\eta_2}
   \] Subbing back \(\mu, \sigma\): \[
   = n\mu^2 + n\sigma^2 = n(\mu^2 + \sigma^2)
   \]
\item
  \textbf{Second Moment (Covariance)} \[
   \text{Cov}(T_1, T_2) = \frac{\partial^2 A}{\partial \eta_1 \partial \eta_2} = \frac{\partial}{\partial \eta_2} \left( -\frac{n\eta_1}{2\eta_2} \right) = \frac{n\eta_1}{2\eta_2^2} = 2n\mu\sigma^2
   \]
\item
  \textbf{Independence of} \(\bar{X}\) and \(S^2\): We verify that
  \(\text{Cov}(\bar{X}, S^2) = 0\). Express \(\bar{X}\) and \(S^2\) in
  terms of \(T_1\) and \(T_2\): \[
   \bar{X} = \frac{1}{n} T_1
   \] \[
   S^2 = \frac{1}{n-1} \left( \sum X_i^2 - n\bar{X}^2 \right) = \frac{1}{n-1} \left( T_2 - \frac{1}{n} T_1^2 \right)
   \]

  Now compute the covariance (ignoring constants \(\frac{1}{n(n-1)}\)
  for now): \[
   \text{Cov}\left(T_1, T_2 - \frac{1}{n}T_1^2\right) = \text{Cov}(T_1, T_2) - \frac{1}{n} \text{Cov}(T_1, T_1^2)
   \] We need \(\text{Cov}(T_1, T_1^2)\). Since
  \(T_1 = \sum X_i \sim N(n\mu, n\sigma^2)\), we use the property of the
  normal distribution that for \(Y \sim N(\theta, \tau^2)\),
  \(\text{Cov}(Y, Y^2) = 2\theta \tau^2\). Here \(\theta = n\mu\) and
  \(\tau^2 = n\sigma^2\): \[
   \text{Cov}(T_1, T_1^2) = 2(n\mu)(n\sigma^2) = 2n^2\mu\sigma^2
   \] Substituting this back into the expression: \[
   \text{Cov}\left(T_1, T_2 - \frac{1}{n}T_1^2\right) = \underbrace{2n\mu\sigma^2}_{\text{From Part 3}} - \frac{1}{n} (2n^2\mu\sigma^2) = 2n\mu\sigma^2 - 2n\mu\sigma^2 = 0
   \] Since \(\bar{X}\) and \(S^2\) are uncorrelated and derived from
  normally distributed data, they are \textbf{independent}.
\end{enumerate}

\end{example}

\paragraph*{\texorpdfstring{Moments of the Curved Normal
\(N(\theta, \theta^2)\)}{Moments of the Curved Normal N(\textbackslash theta, \textbackslash theta\^{}2)}}\label{moments-of-the-curved-normal-ntheta-theta2}
\addcontentsline{toc}{paragraph}{Moments of the Curved Normal
\(N(\theta, \theta^2)\)}

\begin{example}[Moments of the Curved Normal
\(N(\theta, \theta^2)\)]\protect\hypertarget{exm-curved-normal-moments}{}\label{exm-curved-normal-moments}

Consider a sample
\(X_1, \dots, X_n \overset{iid}{\sim} N(\theta, \theta^2)\). This is a
curved exponential family with \(d=1\) parameter and \(k=2\) sufficient
statistics.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Log-Likelihood Components} The log-likelihood is given by:

  \[
  \ell(\theta; \mathbf{x}) = -\frac{1}{2\theta^2} \sum x_i^2 + \frac{1}{\theta} \sum x_i - n \log \theta - \text{const}
  \]

  From this, we identify:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Natural parameters: \(\eta_1(\theta) = -1/(2\theta^2)\) and
    \(\eta_2(\theta) = 1/\theta\).
  \item
    Sufficient statistics: \(T_1 = \sum X_i^2\) and \(T_2 = \sum X_i\).
  \item
    Log-partition function: \(A(\theta) = n \log \theta\).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Applying the Theorem} Since \(d=1\), we have one equation from
  the score identity
  \(\partial A / \partial \theta = \sum (\partial \eta_i / \partial \theta) E[T_i]\):

  \[
  \frac{n}{\theta} = \frac{\partial \eta_1}{\partial \theta} E[T_1] + \frac{\partial \eta_2}{\partial \theta} E[T_2]
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Calculating Derivatives}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(\frac{\partial \eta_1}{\partial \theta} = \frac{\partial}{\partial \theta} (-\frac{1}{2}\theta^{-2}) = \theta^{-3} = \frac{1}{\theta^3}\)
  \item
    \(\frac{\partial \eta_2}{\partial \theta} = \frac{\partial}{\partial \theta} (\theta^{-1}) = -\theta^{-2} = -\frac{1}{\theta^2}\)
  \item
    \(\frac{\partial A}{\partial \theta} = \frac{n}{\theta}\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Solving the System} Substituting these into the equation:

  \[
  \frac{n}{\theta} = \frac{1}{\theta^3} E\left[\sum X_i^2\right] - \frac{1}{\theta^2} E\left[\sum X_i\right]
  \]

  Multiplying by \(\theta^3\) gives:

  \[
  n\theta^2 = E\left[\sum X_i^2\right] - \theta E\left[\sum X_i\right]
  \]

  We can verify this using the known moments \(E[X_i] = \theta\) and
  \(E[X_i^2] = \text{Var}(X) + (E[X])^2 = \theta^2 + \theta^2 = 2\theta^2\):

  \[
  n\theta^2 = (2n\theta^2) - \theta(n\theta) = 2n\theta^2 - n\theta^2 = n\theta^2
  \]

  The identity holds, demonstrating that the theorem correctly relates
  the moments even when the sufficient statistics are ``mixed'' by the
  parameter constraints.
\end{enumerate}

\end{example}

\bookmarksetup{startatroot}

\chapter{Maximum Likelihood
Estimation}\label{maximum-likelihood-estimation}

\section{Maximum Likelihood
Estimation}\label{maximum-likelihood-estimation-1}

\subsection{Definitions and
Notations}\label{definitions-and-notations-1}

\begin{definition}[Maximum Likelihood
Estimation]\protect\hypertarget{def-likelihood-mle}{}\label{def-likelihood-mle}

~

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Likelihood Function:}

  Let \(f(\mathbf{x}|\boldsymbol{\theta})\) be the joint probability
  density function of the data \(\mathbf{X}\). When viewed as a function
  of the parameter \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\) given fixed data
  \(\mathbf{x}\), it is called the likelihood function: \[
   L(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{x}) = f(\mathbf{x}|\boldsymbol{\theta})
   \]
\item
  \textbf{Log-likelihood:}

  It is usually easier to maximize the natural logarithm of the
  likelihood: \[
   \ell(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{x}) = \log L(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{x})
   \]
\item
  \textbf{Maximum Likelihood Estimator (MLE):}

  The MLE \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_{\text{MLE}}\) is the value that
  maximizes the likelihood function: \[
   \hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_{\text{MLE}}(\mathbf{x}) = \operatorname*{argmax}_{\boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta} \ell(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{x})
   \]
\item
  \textbf{Score Function (\(\mathbf{U}\)):}

  The score function is defined as the gradient of the log-likelihood
  with respect to the parameter vector. \[
   \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{x}) = \nabla_{\boldsymbol{\theta}} \ell(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{x})
   \] Finding the MLE often involves solving the score equation: \[
   \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{x}) \equiv 0
   \]
\item
  \textbf{Observed Fisher Information:}

  \[
  \mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{X}) = - \nabla^2 \ell(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{X})
  \]
\item
  \textbf{(Expected) Fisher Information Matrix (\(\mathcal{I}\)):} The
  covariance matrix of the score vector. It is a deterministic
  \(p \times p\) matrix (for a fixed \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\)). \[
   \mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}} \left[ \mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{X}) \right]
   \]
\end{enumerate}

\end{definition}

\subsection{Summary of Key Asymptotic Results for
MLE}\label{summary-of-key-asymptotic-results-for-mle}

Under standard regularity conditions (e.g., i.i.d. observations, smooth
density, and \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\) in the interior of \(\Theta\)), the
MLE exhibits the following asymptotic properties as \(n \to \infty\):

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Consistency:} The estimator converges in probability to the
  true parameter \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\). \[
   \hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_{\text{MLE}} \xrightarrow{p} \boldsymbol{\theta}_0
   \]
\item
  \textbf{Asymptotic Normality of the Score:} The Score vector evaluated
  at the true parameter converges to a Normal distribution with variance
  equal to the Fisher Information. \[
   \frac{1}{\sqrt{n}} \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \xrightarrow{d} N_p(\mathbf{0}, \mathcal{I}_1(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0))
   \] \emph{(Where \(\mathcal{I}_1\) is the expected information for a
  single observation).}
\item
  \textbf{Asymptotic Normality of the Estimator:} The MLE is
  asymptotically normal, unbiased, and efficient (achieving the
  Cramér-Rao lower bound). \[
   \sqrt{n}(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_{\text{MLE}} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \xrightarrow{d} N_p(\mathbf{0}, \mathcal{I}_1(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)^{-1})
   \]
\item
  \textbf{Wilks' Theorem (Likelihood Ratio for Nested Models):} Consider
  two nested models: a \textbf{Full Model} with parameter space
  \(\Theta_{\text{Full}}\) (dimension \(p\)) and a \textbf{Reduced
  Model} restricted to a subspace \(\Theta_{\text{Reduced}}\) (dimension
  \(q < p\)).

  The Likelihood Ratio Statistic (Deviance) is defined as: \[
   D = 2 \left( \ell(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_{\text{Full}}) - \ell(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_{\text{Reduced}}) \right)
   \] Under the null hypothesis that the Reduced Model is true, the
  statistic converges to a Chi-squared distribution: \[
   D \xrightarrow{d} \chi^2_{p-q}
   \] \emph{(The degrees of freedom equal the difference in the number
  of parameters).}
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Examples}\label{examples-3}

\subsubsection{Example: Normal Distribution (Exact
Asymptotics)}\label{example-normal-distribution-exact-asymptotics}

\begin{example}[Example: Normal Distribution (Exact
Asymptotics)]\protect\hypertarget{exm-normal-mle-asymptotics}{}\label{exm-normal-mle-asymptotics}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \overset{iid}{\sim} N(\theta, \sigma^2)\) with
\(\sigma^2\) known. We explicitly derive the key quantities and map them
to the four general asymptotic results.

\textbf{1. Derivation of Key Quantities}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Log-Likelihood:} \[
    \ell(\theta) = -\frac{n}{2}\log(2\pi\sigma^2) - \frac{1}{2\sigma^2}\sum_{i=1}^n (x_i - \theta)^2
    \]
\item
  \textbf{Score Function (\(U_n\)):} Differentiating with respect to
  \(\theta\): \[
    U_n(\theta) = \ell'(\theta) = \frac{1}{\sigma^2}\sum_{i=1}^n (x_i - \theta) = \frac{n}{\sigma^2}(\bar{X}_n - \theta)
    \]
\item
  \textbf{Maximum Likelihood Estimator (\(\hat{\theta}_n\)):} Setting
  \(U_n(\hat{\theta}) = 0\): \[
    \frac{n}{\sigma^2}(\bar{X}_n - \hat{\theta}) = 0 \implies \hat{\theta}_n = \bar{X}_n
    \]
\item
  \textbf{Fisher Information (\(\mathcal{I}_n\)):} Taking the negative
  second derivative: \[
    \mathcal{I}_n(\theta) = -E[\ell''(\theta)] = -E\left[ -\frac{n}{\sigma^2} \right] = \frac{n}{\sigma^2}
    \] \emph{(Note: For a single observation,
  \(\mathcal{I}_1 = 1/\sigma^2\)).}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{2. Asymptotic Properties}

We now illustrate the four main theorems using these derived forms:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Consistency}

  The MLE is \(\hat{\theta}_n = \bar{X}_n\). By the Weak Law of Large
  Numbers, we know that: \[
  \bar{X}_n \xrightarrow{p} \theta
  \] Thus, the estimator is consistent.
\item
  \textbf{Asymptotic Normality of the Score}

  The score function is
  \(U_n(\theta) = \frac{n}{\sigma^2}(\bar{X}_n - \theta)\). Since
  \(\bar{X}_n\) is normally distributed, the Score is \textbf{exactly}
  Normal for any \(n\): \[
  U_n(\theta) \sim N\left(0, \frac{n}{\sigma^2}\right) = N(0, \mathcal{I}_n)
  \] Scaling by \(1/\sqrt{n}\), we recover the asymptotic result: \[
  \frac{1}{\sqrt{n}} U_n(\theta) \sim N(0, \mathcal{I}_1)
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Asymptotic Normality of the Estimator}

  The distribution of the standardized MLE is derived from the
  properties of the sample mean: \[
  \sqrt{n}(\hat{\theta}_n - \theta) = \sqrt{n}(\bar{X}_n - \theta) \sim N(0, \sigma^2)
  \] Note that \(\sigma^2 = 1/\mathcal{I}_1\). Therefore: \[
  \sqrt{n}(\hat{\theta}_n - \theta) \sim N(0, \mathcal{I}_1^{-1})
  \] Again, the asymptotic approximation holds exactly for finite sample
  sizes here.
\item
  \textbf{Wilks' Theorem (Deviance Approximation)}

  We test \(H_0: \theta = \theta_0\). The Deviance is
  \(D = 2[\ell(\hat{\theta}) - \ell(\theta_0)]\).

  \textbf{Step A: Taylor Expansion} We expand the log-likelihood around
  the MLE. In general, this is an approximation, but for the Normal
  distribution (where the log-likelihood is exactly quadratic), the
  expansion is \textbf{exact}: \[
  \ell(\theta_0) = \ell(\hat{\theta}) + (\theta_0 - \hat{\theta}) \underbrace{\ell'(\hat{\theta})}_{0} + \frac{1}{2}(\theta_0 - \hat{\theta})^2 \ell''(\hat{\theta})
  \] Substituting the constant curvature
  \(\ell''(\theta) = -n/\sigma^2\): \[
  2[\ell(\hat{\theta}) - \ell(\theta_0)] = -(\theta_0 - \hat{\theta})^2 \left( -\frac{n}{\sigma^2} \right) = \frac{n}{\sigma^2}(\hat{\theta} - \theta_0)^2
  \]

  \textbf{Step B: Exactness in the Normal Case} Because the
  log-likelihood for the Normal mean is a perfect quadratic (a
  parabola), the third derivatives are zero. Therefore, the Taylor
  approximation is \textbf{exact}: \[
  D = \frac{n}{\sigma^2}(\hat{\theta} - \theta_0)^2
  \]

  \textbf{Step C: Equivalence of Forms
  (\(D = \text{Wald} = \text{Score}\))}

  Let \(\Delta \hat \theta = \hat{\theta} - \theta_0\) be the estimation
  error. We can rewrite the exact Deviance derived above in terms of the
  Information (\(\mathcal{I}_n = n/\sigma^2\)) and the Score at the null
  (\(U_0 = \mathcal{I}_n \Delta \hat \theta\)).

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \textbf{Deviance Form:} From Step B, we see directly: \[
      D = \left( \frac{n}{\sigma^2} \right) (\hat{\theta} - \theta_0)^2 = \mathcal{I}_n (\Delta \hat \theta)^2
      \]
  \item
    \textbf{Score Statistic Form:} The Score at the null hypothesis is
    \(U(\theta_0) = \mathcal{I}_n (\hat{\theta} - \theta_0)\). \[
      U^T \mathcal{I}^{-1} U = [\mathcal{I}_n \Delta \hat \theta] \cdot [\mathcal{I}_n^{-1}] \cdot [\mathcal{I}_n \Delta \hat \theta] = \Delta \hat \theta \cdot \mathcal{I}_n \cdot \Delta \hat \theta = \mathcal{I}_n (\Delta \hat \theta)^2
      \]
  \item
    \textbf{Result:} In the Normal case (perfect quadratic likelihood),
    the three statistics are identical: \[
      \boxed{ D = \Delta \hat \theta^T \mathcal{I}_n \Delta \hat \theta = U_n(\theta_0)^T \mathcal{I}_n^{-1} U_n(\theta_0) }
      \]
  \end{itemize}

  \textbf{Step D: Conclusion} The term inside the Deviance is the
  standard Z-statistic squared (\(Z \sim N(0,1)\)): \[
  D = \left( \frac{\bar{X}_n - \theta_0}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}} \right)^2 = Z^2 \sim \chi^2_1
  \] This illustrates Wilks' theorem: the deviance statistic converges
  to a Chi-squared distribution (degrees of freedom = 1).
\end{enumerate}

\end{example}

\subsubsection{Uniform Distribution (Boundary
Case)}\label{uniform-distribution-boundary-case}

\begin{example}[]\protect\hypertarget{exm-uniform-mle-boundary}{}\label{exm-uniform-mle-boundary}

Let
\(X_1, \dots, X_n \overset{iid}{\sim} \operatorname{\text{Unif}}(0, \theta)\).
The likelihood is: \[
L(\theta; \mathbf{x}) = \frac{1}{\theta^n} I(x_{(n)} \le \theta)
\] This function strictly decreases for \(\theta \ge x_{(n)}\) and is
zero otherwise. Thus: \[
\hat{\theta}_{\text{MLE}} = x_{(n)}
\] Note that the Score equation approach fails here because the support
depends on \(\theta\), making the log-likelihood discontinuous at the
boundary.

\end{example}

\section{Optimization Algorithms for Finding
MLE}\label{optimization-algorithms-for-finding-mle}

When the score equation \(\mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = \mathbf{0}\)
does not have a closed-form solution, we must rely on iterative
numerical methods to find the Maximum Likelihood Estimator. The
Newton-Raphson algorithm is the most common approach, utilizing the
curvature of the log-likelihood surface to guide the search for the
maximum.

\subsection{Newton-Raphson Iteration}\label{newton-raphson-iteration}

\begin{algorithm}

Starting from an initial guess \(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(0)}\), the
algorithm updates the estimate of the parameter vector at each step
\(t\) using the following recurrence relation:

\[
\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t+1)} = \boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)} + \left[ \mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)}) \right]^{-1} \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)})
\]

where:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{\(\mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)})\)} is the score vector
  (gradient) evaluated at the current estimate.
\item
  \textbf{\(\mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)})\)} is the
  \textbf{Observed Fisher Information Matrix}, defined as the negative
  Hessian of the log-likelihood:

  \[
  \mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = - \nabla^2 \ell(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = - \frac{\partial^2 \ell(\boldsymbol{\theta})}{\partial \boldsymbol{\theta} \partial \boldsymbol{\theta}^T}
  \]
\end{enumerate}

\end{algorithm}

\begin{proof}[Derivation via Taylor Expansion]
The algorithm is derived by approximating the score function
\(\mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) with a first-order Taylor expansion
around the current estimate \(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)}\):

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Linear Approximation} \n

  We approximate the score at the next step as:

  \[
  \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t+1)}) \approx \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)}) + \nabla \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)}) (\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t+1)} - \boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)})
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Setting to Zero} \n

  Since we want to find the root where
  \(\mathbf{U}(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}) = \mathbf{0}\), we set the
  left side to zero:

  \[
  \mathbf{0} = \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)}) - \mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)}) (\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t+1)} - \boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)})
  \]

  Note that
  \(\nabla \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = \nabla^2 \ell(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = -\mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta})\).
\item
  \textbf{Solving for the Update} \n

  Rearranging the terms gives the update rule:

  \[
  \mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)}) (\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t+1)} - \boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)}) = \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)})
  \]

  \[
  \boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t+1)} = \boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)} + \mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)})^{-1} \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)})
  \]
\end{enumerate}

\end{proof}

\subsection{Fisher Scoring}\label{fisher-scoring}

\begin{algorithm}

The Newton-Raphson algorithm specifically uses the \textbf{Observed
Information} \(\mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta})\). If the expected Fisher
Information
\(\mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = E[\mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta})]\)
is used instead, the method is known as \textbf{Fisher Scoring}.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Convergence Speed} \n

  Newton-Raphson typically achieves quadratic convergence near the
  maximum, making it very fast when the initial guess is good.
\item
  \textbf{Stability} \n

  Because \(\mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) depends on the specific
  data observed, it can occasionally be non-positive definite in regions
  far from the MLE, which may cause the algorithm to diverge. Fisher
  Scoring is often more stable in these cases because
  \(\mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) is always positive semi-definite
  for regular families.
\end{enumerate}

\end{algorithm}

\subsection{Example: Poisson Regression
(MLE)}\label{example-poisson-regression-mle}

\begin{example}[]\protect\hypertarget{exm-poisson-glm}{}\label{exm-poisson-glm}

Consider a GLM where \(Y_i \sim \text{Poisson}(\lambda_i)\). We use the
canonical link
\(\log(\lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta})) = \mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta}\),
which implies the mean function is
\(\lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \exp(\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta})\).

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Exponential Family Representation}

  We rewrite the total log-likelihood by grouping terms associated with
  each \(\beta_j\):

  \[
   \begin{aligned}
   \ell(\boldsymbol{\beta}) &= \sum_{i=1}^n \left( y_i (\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta}) - \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta}) - \log(y_i!) \right) \\
   &= \sum_{i=1}^n \sum_{j=0}^p y_i x_{ij} \beta_j - \sum_{i=1}^n \exp(\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta}) - \sum_{i=1}^n \log(y_i!) \\
   &= \sum_{j=0}^p \beta_j \underbrace{\left( \sum_{i=1}^n y_i x_{ij} \right)}_{T_j(\mathbf{y})} - \underbrace{\sum_{i=1}^n \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta})}_{A(\boldsymbol{\beta})} + \text{const}
   \end{aligned}
   \]

  This is a multivariate exponential family in \textbf{canonical form}
  where:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \textbf{Natural Parameters:} The regression coefficients
    \(\boldsymbol{\beta} = (\beta_0, \dots, \beta_p)^\top\).
  \item
    \textbf{Sufficient Statistics:}
    \(\mathbf{T}(\mathbf{y}) = \mathbf{X}^\top \mathbf{y}\).
  \item
    \textbf{Log-Partition Function:}
    \(A(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \sum_{i=1}^n \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta})\).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Derivations via Moments of Sufficient Statistics}

  Since we are in canonical form, the Score and Information are simply
  the derivatives of the log-partition function
  \(A(\boldsymbol{\beta})\).

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \textbf{Score Vector (\(\mathbf{U}\)):} \[
      \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \mathbf{T}(\mathbf{y}) - \nabla A(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \mathbf{X}^\top \mathbf{y} - \mathbf{X}^\top \boldsymbol{\lambda}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \mathbf{X}^\top (\mathbf{y} - \boldsymbol{\lambda}(\boldsymbol{\beta}))
      \] where
    \(\boldsymbol{\lambda}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = (\lambda_1(\boldsymbol{\beta}), \dots, \lambda_n(\boldsymbol{\beta}))^\top\).
  \item
    \textbf{Fisher Information (\(J\)):} The information is the Hessian
    of the log-partition function: \[
      J(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \nabla^2 A(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \mathbf{X}^\top \mathbf{W}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) \mathbf{X}
      \] where
    \(\mathbf{W}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \text{diag}(\lambda_1(\boldsymbol{\beta}), \dots, \lambda_n(\boldsymbol{\beta}))\).
  \end{itemize}

  The details of procedure for deriving the above formula for score and
  fisher information matrix using scalar derivative is given
  Example~\ref{exm-poisson-score-fisher}.
\item
  \textbf{Newton-Raphson Algorithm for Poisson MLE}

  To find the MLE \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}}\), we iterate until
  convergence:

  \[
   \boldsymbol{\beta}^{(t+1)} = \boldsymbol{\beta}^{(t)} + [J(\boldsymbol{\beta}^{(t)})]^{-1} \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\beta}^{(t)})
   \]

  Substituting the Poisson-specific forms:

  \[
   \boldsymbol{\beta}^{(t+1)} = \boldsymbol{\beta}^{(t)} + (\mathbf{X}^\top \mathbf{W}(\boldsymbol{\beta}^{(t)}) \mathbf{X})^{-1} \mathbf{X}^\top (\mathbf{y} - \boldsymbol{\lambda}(\boldsymbol{\beta}^{(t)}))
   \]

  This specific iteration is also known as \textbf{Iteratively
  Reweighted Least Squares (IRLS)}.
\end{enumerate}

\end{example}

\section{Review of Convergence Theorems for
Probability}\label{review-of-convergence-theorems-for-probability}

\begin{theorem}[Weak Law of Large Numbers
(WLLN)]\protect\hypertarget{thm-lln}{}\label{thm-lln}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n\) be independent and identically distributed
(i.i.d.) random variables with mean \(E[X_i] = \mu\) and finite variance
\(\text{Var}(X_i) = \sigma^2 < \infty\).

Then, the sample mean \(\bar{X}_n = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n X_i\)
converges in probability to \(\mu\): \[
\bar{X}_n \xrightarrow{p} \mu \quad \text{as } n \to \infty
\] Formal definition: For any \(\epsilon > 0\),
\(\lim_{n \to \infty} P(|\bar{X}_n - \mu| > \epsilon) = 0\).

\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Central Limit Theorem for IID
Cases]\protect\hypertarget{thm-clt}{}\label{thm-clt}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n\) be i.i.d. random variables with mean
\(E[X_i] = \mu\) and finite variance
\(0 < \text{Var}(X_i) = \sigma^2 < \infty\).

Then, the random variable \(\sqrt{n}(\bar{X}_n - \mu)\) converges in
distribution to a normal distribution with mean 0 and variance
\(\sigma^2\): \[
\sqrt{n}(\bar{X}_n - \mu) \xrightarrow{d} N(0, \sigma^2)
\]

\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Lindeberg-Feller CLT (For Non-Identical
Distributions)]\protect\hypertarget{thm-lindeberg-feller}{}\label{thm-lindeberg-feller}

This variation is crucial for regression analysis (e.g., OLS properties
with fixed regressors) where variables are independent but \textbf{not}
identically distributed.

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n\) be independent random variables with
\(E[X_i] = \mu_i\) and \(\text{Var}(X_i) = \sigma_i^2\). Define the
\textbf{average variance} \(\tilde{\sigma}_n^2\): \[
\tilde{\sigma}_n^2 = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n \sigma_i^2
\]

If the \textbf{Lindeberg Condition} holds: For every \(\epsilon > 0\),
\[
\lim_{n \to \infty} \frac{1}{n \tilde{\sigma}_n^2} \sum_{i=1}^n E\left[ (X_i - \mu_i)^2 \cdot I\left( |X_i - \mu_i| > \epsilon \sqrt{n \tilde{\sigma}_n^2} \right) \right] = 0
\]

Then the standardized sum converges to a standard normal: \[
\frac{\sum_{i=1}^n (X_i - \mu_i)}{\sqrt{n \tilde{\sigma}_n^2}} \xrightarrow{d} N(0, 1)
\]

\end{theorem}

\begin{corollary}[Approximating Distribution for Sample Mean
(Non-i.i.d.)]\protect\hypertarget{cor-lindeberg-feller-approx}{}\label{cor-lindeberg-feller-approx}

Under the conditions of the Lindeberg-Feller CLT, we can derive the
asymptotic distribution for the sample mean
\(\bar{X}_n = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n X_i\).

Let \(\bar{\mu}_n = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n \mu_i\) be the average mean.
Note that the denominator in the CLT is simply
\(\sqrt{n} \tilde{\sigma}_n\).

The standardized sum converges to \(N(0,1)\): \[
\frac{\sum (X_i - \mu_i)}{\sqrt{n \tilde{\sigma}_n^2}} = \frac{n(\bar{X}_n - \bar{\mu}_n)}{\sqrt{n} \tilde{\sigma}_n} = \frac{\sqrt{n}(\bar{X}_n - \bar{\mu}_n)}{\tilde{\sigma}_n} \xrightarrow{d} N(0, 1)
\]

This implies the following \textbf{approximate distributions} for large
\(n\):

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{For the Sample Mean:}

  \[
   \bar{X}_n \overset{\cdot}{\sim} N\left(\bar{\mu}_n, \frac{\tilde{\sigma}_n^2}{n}\right)
   \]
\item
  \textbf{For the Scaled Difference (Root-n consistency):}

  \[
   \sqrt{n}(\bar{X}_n - \bar{\mu}_n) \overset{\cdot}{\sim} N\left(0, \tilde{\sigma}_n^2\right)
   \]
\end{enumerate}

\emph{Note: If all \(X_i\) share the same mean \(\mu\), simply replace
\(\bar{\mu}_n\) with \(\mu\).}

\end{corollary}

\begin{theorem}[Slutsky's
Theorem]\protect\hypertarget{thm-slutsky}{}\label{thm-slutsky}

Let \(X_n\) and \(Y_n\) be sequences of random variables. If
\(X_n \xrightarrow{d} X\) and \(Y_n \xrightarrow{p} c\), where \(c\) is
a constant, then:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Sum:} \(X_n + Y_n \xrightarrow{d} X + c\)
\item
  \textbf{Product:} \(X_n Y_n \xrightarrow{d} cX\)
\item
  \textbf{Quotient:} \(X_n / Y_n \xrightarrow{d} X/c\) (provided
  \(c \ne 0\))
\end{enumerate}

\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Generalized Slutsky's Theorem (Continuous
Mapping)]\protect\hypertarget{thm-generalized-slutsky}{}\label{thm-generalized-slutsky}

The arithmetic operations in Slutsky's theorem are special cases of a
broader property.

Let \(X_n \xrightarrow{d} X\) and \(Y_n \xrightarrow{p} c\), where \(c\)
is a constant. Let \(g: \mathbb{R}^2 \to \mathbb{R}^k\) be a function
that is \textbf{continuous} at every point \((x, c)\) where \(x\) is in
the support of \(X\).

Then: \[
g(X_n, Y_n) \xrightarrow{d} g(X, c)
\]

This implies that for any ``well-behaved'' algebraic combination
(polynomials, exponentials, etc.) of a sequence converging in
distribution and a sequence converging in probability to a constant, the
limit behaves as if the constant were substituted directly.

\end{theorem}

\begin{example}[Asymptotic Normality of Sample
Variance]\protect\hypertarget{exm-asymptotic-normal-variance}{}\label{exm-asymptotic-normal-variance}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \overset{iid}{\sim} N(\mu, \sigma^2)\). We wish to
derive the asymptotic distribution of the MLE for variance,

\[\hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n (X_i - \bar{X})^2.\]

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Algebraic Expansion

  We rewrite the estimator by adding and subtracting the true mean
  \(\mu\): \[
   \sum_{i=1}^n (X_i - \bar{X})^2 = \sum_{i=1}^n ((X_i - \mu) - (\bar{X} - \mu))^2
   \] \[
   = \sum_{i=1}^n (X_i - \mu)^2 - 2(\bar{X} - \mu)\sum_{i=1}^n(X_i - \mu) + n(\bar{X} - \mu)^2
   \] Since \(\sum(X_i - \mu) = n(\bar{X} - \mu)\): \[
   = \sum_{i=1}^n (X_i - \mu)^2 - n(\bar{X} - \mu)^2
   \] Dividing by \(n\), we get: \[
   \hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n (X_i - \mu)^2 - (\bar{X} - \mu)^2
   \]
\item
  Scaling by \(\sqrt{n}\)

  We rearrange to look at the pivotal quantity
  \(\sqrt{n}(\hat{\sigma}^2 - \sigma^2)\): \[
   \sqrt{n}(\hat{\sigma}^2 - \sigma^2) = \sqrt{n}\left( \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n (X_i - \mu)^2 - \sigma^2 \right) - \sqrt{n}(\bar{X} - \mu)^2
   \]
\item
  Applying Convergence Theorems

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \textbf{Term 1 (CLT):}

    Let \(W_i = (X_i - \mu)^2\). Since \(X_i \sim N(\mu, \sigma^2)\),
    \(W_i/\sigma^2 \sim \chi^2_1\). moments of \(W_i\):
    \(E[W_i] = \sigma^2\) and \(\text{Var}(W_i) = 2\sigma^4\). By the
    standard CLT: \[
      \sqrt{n}(\bar{W} - E[W]) = \sqrt{n}\left( \frac{1}{n}\sum (X_i - \mu)^2 - \sigma^2 \right) \xrightarrow{d} N(0, 2\sigma^4)
      \]
  \item
    \textbf{Term 2 (Slutsky):}

    Consider the term \(\sqrt{n}(\bar{X} - \mu)^2\). We can rewrite this
    as: \[
      \underbrace{\sqrt{n}(\bar{X} - \mu)}_{\xrightarrow{d} N(0, \sigma^2)} \cdot \underbrace{(\bar{X} - \mu)}_{\xrightarrow{p} 0 \text{ by LLN}}
      \] By Slutsky's Theorem, the product converges to
    \(Z \cdot 0 = 0\).
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Conclusion:}

Combining the terms: \[
\sqrt{n}(\hat{\sigma}^2 - \sigma^2) \xrightarrow{d} N(0, 2\sigma^4) - 0 = N(0, 2\sigma^4)
\]

\end{example}

\section{Asymptotic Theory of Maximized
Likelihood}\label{asymptotic-theory-of-maximized-likelihood}

\subsection{Consistency of the MLE}\label{consistency-of-the-mle}

\subsubsection{IID Cases}\label{iid-cases}

Consistency establishes that as the sample size grows, the estimator
converges in probability to the true parameter value.

\begin{lemma}[Minimization of KL Divergence at the True
Density]\protect\hypertarget{lem-kl-divergence-mle}{}\label{lem-kl-divergence-mle}

Let \(f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\) be the true density of a random
variable \(Y\), and let \(f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta})\) be any other
density in the family. The \textbf{Kullback-Leibler (KL) Divergence} (or
relative entropy) between the true distribution and a candidate
distribution is defined as:

\[
D_{KL}(f(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \| f(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta})) = E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0} \left[ \log \left( \frac{f(Y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)}{f(Y|\boldsymbol{\theta})} \right) \right]
\]

This quantity satisfies the \textbf{Information Inequality}:

\[
D_{KL}(f(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \| f(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta})) \ge 0
\]

with equality if and only if
\(f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta}) = f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\) almost
everywhere. This implies that the expected log-likelihood
\(M(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0}[\log f(Y|\boldsymbol{\theta})]\)
is uniquely maximized at the true parameter \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\)
(or true density for \(Y\)).

\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\leavevmode

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Information Inequality via Jensen's Inequality}

  Consider the negative KL divergence, which is the expected
  log-likelihood ratio. By \textbf{Jensen's Inequality}, since the
  logarithm is a strictly concave function:

  \[
  -D_{KL}(f(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \| f(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta})) = E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0} \left[ \log \left( \frac{f(Y|\boldsymbol{\theta})}{f(Y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)} \right) \right] \le \log E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0} \left[ \frac{f(Y|\boldsymbol{\theta})}{f(Y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)} \right]
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Evaluating the Expectation}

  We evaluate the expectation on the right-hand side:

  \[
  E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0} \left[ \frac{f(Y|\boldsymbol{\theta})}{f(Y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)} \right] = \int_{\mathcal{Y}} \left( \frac{f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta})}{f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)} \right) f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \, dy = \int_{\mathcal{Y}} f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta}) \, dy = 1
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Conclusion}

  Substituting the result into the inequality:

  \[
  -D_{KL}(f(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \| f(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta})) \le \log(1) = 0 \implies D_{KL}(f(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \| f(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta})) \ge 0
  \]

  Because the log function is strictly concave, the equality
  \(E[\log Z] = \log E[Z]\) holds if and only if \(Z\) is a constant
  with probability 1. Here,
  \(Z = f(Y|\boldsymbol{\theta})/f(Y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\), so
  equality holds if and only if
  \(f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta}) = f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\) for almost
  all \(y\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Consistency of
MLE]\protect\hypertarget{thm-mle-consistency}{}\label{thm-mle-consistency}

Let \(Y_1, \dots, Y_n\) be i.i.d. with density
\(f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta})\). Let \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\) be the true
parameter. Assume the following \textbf{Identifiability} condition
holds:

\[
f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta}) = f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \text{ for almost all } y \implies \boldsymbol{\theta} = \boldsymbol{\theta}_0
\]

Under this and other standard regularity conditions (such as compactness
of the parameter space \(\Theta\)), the Maximum Likelihood Estimator
\(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n\) satisfies:

\[
\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n \xrightarrow{p} \boldsymbol{\theta}_0 \quad \text{as } n \to \infty
\]

\end{theorem}

\begin{refremark}[The Role of Identifiability]
Identifiability is a fundamental requirement for consistency because it
ensures that different parameter values correspond to different
probability distributions.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Uniqueness of the Mapping}

  Mathematically, it means the mapping
  \(\boldsymbol{\theta} \to f(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta})\) is
  one-to-one. If the model were not identifiable, there would exist at
  least one \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_1 \neq \boldsymbol{\theta}_0\) such
  that \(f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_1) = f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\).
\item
  \textbf{Uniqueness of the Maximum}

  In the context of the proof, Lemma~\ref{lem-kl-divergence-mle} shows
  that the KL divergence is zero if and only if the densities are
  identical. Identifiability allows us to move from the space of
  ``densities'' back to the space of ``parameters,'' ensuring that
  \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\) is the \textbf{unique} global maximum of the
  expected log-likelihood function \(M(\boldsymbol{\theta})\).
\item
  \textbf{Failure of Consistency}

  Without identifiability, the data cannot distinguish between
  \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\) and \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_1\). The MLE would
  not converge to a single point but rather to the set of all parameters
  that produce the true density.
\end{enumerate}

\label{rem-identifiability}

\end{refremark}

\begin{proof}
\leavevmode

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{The Objective Function}

  The MLE maximizes the average log-likelihood:

  \[
  M_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n \log f(Y_i|\boldsymbol{\theta})
  \]

  By the \textbf{Weak Law of Large Numbers (WLLN)}, for any fixed
  \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\), \(M_n(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) converges in
  probability to its expectation:

  \[
  M_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}) \xrightarrow{p} M(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0} [\log f(Y|\boldsymbol{\theta})]
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Convergence to the Unique Maximum}

  According to Lemma~\ref{lem-kl-divergence-mle}, the limit function
  \(M(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) is maximized when the KL divergence is
  zero. This happens uniquely at \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\) due to
  identifiability.

  Since \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n\) is the maximizer of
  \(M_n(\boldsymbol{\theta})\), and \(M_n(\boldsymbol{\theta})\)
  converges uniformly to \(M(\boldsymbol{\theta})\), the sequence of
  maximizers must converge to the maximizer of the limit function:

  \[
  \hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n \xrightarrow{p} \boldsymbol{\theta}_0
  \]
\end{enumerate}

\end{proof}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{mle_files/figure-pdf/fig-likelihood-concentration-normal-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-likelihood-concentration-normal}Consistency:
Concentration of the Average Log-Likelihood for \(N(\theta, 1)\)}

\end{figure}%

\subsubsection{Non-IID Cases}\label{non-iid-cases}

In regression settings, observations are independent but not identically
distributed (i.n.i.d.). We generalize the consistency result by
requiring that the ``average'' information accumulates sufficiently.

\begin{theorem}[Consistency of MLE (General
Case)]\protect\hypertarget{thm-mle-consistency-general}{}\label{thm-mle-consistency-general}

Let \(Y_1, \dots, Y_n\) be independent observations with densities
\(f_i(y|\boldsymbol{\theta})\) (e.g., depending on covariates \(x_i\)).
Let \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\) be the true parameter.

Under the following conditions:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Parameter Space:} Compact parameter space \(\Theta\).
\item
  \textbf{Identification:} For any
  \(\boldsymbol{\theta} \neq \boldsymbol{\theta}_0\), the average
  Kullback-Leibler divergence is strictly positive in the limit:

  \[\liminf_{n \to \infty} \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n \text{KL}(f_i(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) || f_i(\cdot|\boldsymbol{\theta})) > 0\]
\item
  \textbf{Uniform Convergence:} The log-likelihood satisfies a Uniform
  Law of Large Numbers (ULLN).
\end{enumerate}

Then
\(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n \xrightarrow{p} \boldsymbol{\theta}_0\).

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\leavevmode

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{The Objective Function}

  The MLE maximizes the average log-likelihood: \[
  M_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n \log f_i(Y_i|\boldsymbol{\theta})
  \] Unlike the i.i.d. case, the terms in the sum have different
  expectations. We rely on a \textbf{WLLN for Independent Variables}
  (e.g., Chebyshev's WLLN). Provided the variances are bounded,
  \(M_n(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) converges in probability to the
  \textbf{average expectation}: \[
  M_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}) \xrightarrow{p} \bar{M}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0} [\log f_i(Y_i|\boldsymbol{\theta})]
  \] \emph{Note: For consistency of the maximizer, we strictly require
  \textbf{Uniform Convergence} over \(\Theta\), not just pointwise
  convergence.}
\item
  \textbf{Identifying the Maximum (Average KL Divergence)}

  We compare the limit function at \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\) versus
  \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\). Consider the average difference: \[
  \bar{M}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}) - \bar{M}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0} \left[ \log \left( \frac{f_i(Y_i|\boldsymbol{\theta})}{f_i(Y_i|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)} \right) \right]
  \] By Jensen's Inequality applied to \emph{each} term in the sum: \[
  E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0} \left[ \log \frac{f_i(Y_i|\boldsymbol{\theta})}{f_i(Y_i|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)} \right] \le \log E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0} \left[ \frac{f_i(Y_i|\boldsymbol{\theta})}{f_i(Y_i|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)} \right] = \log(1) = 0
  \] Summing these inequalities implies that
  \(\bar{M}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) is uniquely maximized at
  \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\) (provided the identification condition
  holds). Since the sample function \(M_n(\boldsymbol{\theta})\)
  converges uniformly to this maximized limit function, the argmax
  \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n\) converges to
  \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{proof}

\subsection{Asymptotic Normality of the Score
Vector}\label{asymptotic-normality-of-the-score-vector}

The Score function acts as the ``engine'' for the normality of the MLE.
We treat the I.I.D. and non-I.I.D. cases separately.

\subsubsection{IID Cases}\label{iid-cases-1}

\begin{theorem}[Normality of Score
(I.I.D.)]\protect\hypertarget{thm-score-normality-iid}{}\label{thm-score-normality-iid}

Let \(Y_1, \dots, Y_n\) be i.i.d. with density
\(f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta})\). Define the \textbf{Fisher Information
matrix} for a single observation as the expected outer product of the
score: \[
\mathcal{I}_1(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = E\left[ \left( \nabla_{\boldsymbol{\theta}} \log f(Y_1|\boldsymbol{\theta}) \right) \left( \nabla_{\boldsymbol{\theta}} \log f(Y_1|\boldsymbol{\theta}) \right)^T \right]
\] Then, the scaled total score vector converges to a Normal
distribution: \[
\frac{1}{\sqrt{n}} \mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0; \mathbf{Y}) \xrightarrow{d} N(\mathbf{0}, \mathcal{I}_1(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0))
\]

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The total score is the sum of independent score contributions: \[
\mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0; \mathbf{Y}) = \sum_{i=1}^n \nabla \log f(Y_i|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) = \sum_{i=1}^n \mathbf{u}_i
\] From \textbf{Bartlett's Identities}, for each observation \(i\):

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Mean}

  \(E[\mathbf{u}_i] = \mathbf{0}\).
\item
  \textbf{Variance}

  \(\text{Var}(\mathbf{u}_i) = E[\mathbf{u}_i \mathbf{u}_i^T] = \mathcal{I}_1(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\).
\end{enumerate}

Since the terms \(\mathbf{u}_i\) are i.i.d. with finite variance, we
apply the \textbf{Multivariate Central Limit Theorem (Lindeberg-Lévy)}:
\[
\frac{1}{\sqrt{n}} \sum_{i=1}^n \mathbf{u}_i \xrightarrow{d} N(\mathbf{0}, \text{Var}(\mathbf{u}_i)) = N(\mathbf{0}, \mathcal{I}_1(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0))
\]
\end{proof}

\subsubsection{Case B: The Non-I.I.D. Case (Regression
Setting)}\label{case-b-the-non-i.i.d.-case-regression-setting}

\begin{theorem}[Normality of Score (Independent,
Non-Identical)]\protect\hypertarget{thm-score-normality-noniid}{}\label{thm-score-normality-noniid}

Let \(Y_1, \dots, Y_n\) be independent but not necessarily identically
distributed (e.g., due to different covariates). Let
\(\mathcal{I}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) = \sum_{i=1}^n \text{Var}(\mathbf{u}_i)\)
be the total information.

Define the \textbf{Average Fisher Information Matrix}: \[
\bar{\mathcal{I}}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) = \frac{1}{n} \mathcal{I}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n E[\mathbf{u}_i \mathbf{u}_i^T]
\]

If the \textbf{Lindeberg Condition} is satisfied for the sequence of
score vectors, then the standardized score converges to a standard
Normal: \[
\left( \mathcal{I}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \right)^{-1/2} \mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0; \mathbf{Y}) \xrightarrow{d} N(\mathbf{0}, \mathbf{I}_p)
\] \emph{(Here \(\mathbf{I}_p\) denotes the Identity matrix).}

\textbf{Approximating Distribution:} If the average information
converges to a positive definite limit
\(\bar{\mathcal{I}}_n \to \mathcal{I}_{\infty}\), we have the following
approximation for the scaled average score
\(\sqrt{n} \bar{\mathbf{U}}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0; \mathbf{Y})\): \[
\sqrt{n} \bar{\mathbf{U}}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0; \mathbf{Y}) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{n}} \mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0; \mathbf{Y}) \overset{\cdot}{\sim} N(\mathbf{0}, \bar{\mathcal{I}}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0))
\]

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
This is a direct application of the \textbf{Lindeberg-Feller Central
Limit Theorem}. The total score
\(\mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0; \mathbf{Y}) = \sum \mathbf{u}_i\)
is a sum of independent random vectors with mean \(\mathbf{0}\) and
variances \(\text{Var}(\mathbf{u}_i)\). Provided that no single
observation's score contribution dominates the sum (the Lindeberg
condition), the standardized sum converges to a standard Normal
distribution.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Asymptotic Normality of the
MLE}\label{asymptotic-normality-of-the-mle}

We now transfer the normality from the Score function to the estimator
\(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}\) using a Taylor expansion (the Delta Method
logic).

\begin{definition}[Regularity Conditions for Asymptotic
Normality]\protect\hypertarget{def-regularity-conditions}{}\label{def-regularity-conditions}

The following conditions are required to ensure the validity of the
Taylor expansion and the convergence of the remainder term:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Interiority:} The true parameter \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\)
  lies in the interior of the parameter space \(\Theta\).
\item
  \textbf{Smoothness:} The log-likelihood function
  \(\log f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta})\) is three times continuously
  differentiable with respect to \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\).
\item
  \textbf{Boundedness:} The third derivatives are bounded by an
  integrable function \(M(y)\) (to control the Taylor remainder).
\item
  \textbf{Positive Information:} The Fisher Information Matrix
  \(\mathcal{I}_1(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\) exists and is non-singular
  (positive definite).
\item
  \textbf{Interchangeability:} Differentiation and integration can be
  interchanged for the density \(f(y|\boldsymbol{\theta})\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Asymptotic Normality of
MLE]\protect\hypertarget{thm-mle-normality}{}\label{thm-mle-normality}

Under the regularity conditions above, the MLE
\(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n\) satisfies: \[
\sqrt{n}(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \xrightarrow{d} N\left(\mathbf{0}, [\mathcal{I}_1(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)]^{-1}\right)
\]

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\leavevmode

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Taylor Expansion of Score Equation}

  We expand the Score function \(\mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta})\)
  around the true parameter \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\). Since
  \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n\) is the MLE,
  \(\mathbf{U}_n(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n) = \mathbf{0}\).

  Define the \textbf{Observed Information Matrix} as the negative
  Hessian: \[
  \mathbf{J}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = -\nabla^2 \ell_n(\boldsymbol{\theta})
  \]

  \begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, opacityback=0, colback=white, toprule=.15mm, colframe=quarto-callout-note-color-frame, bottomrule=.15mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, rightrule=.15mm, breakable, left=2mm]
  \begin{minipage}[t]{5.5mm}
  \textcolor{quarto-callout-note-color}{\faInfo}
  \end{minipage}%
  \begin{minipage}[t]{\textwidth - 5.5mm}

  \textbf{The Linear Link (Score vs.~Parameter Error)} The fundamental
  approximation linking the random Score vector to the estimation error
  is: \[
  \underbrace{\mathbf{U}_n(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n)}_{=\mathbf{0}} \approx \mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) - \mathbf{J}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) (\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0)
  \] \[
  \implies \quad \mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \approx \mathbf{J}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) (\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0)
  \]

  \end{minipage}%
  \end{tcolorbox}
\item
  \textbf{Scaling and Inversion}

  Multiply the link equation by \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{n}}\) and introduce
  \(n\) to the Observed Information term to stabilize the limits: \[
  \frac{1}{\sqrt{n}} \mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \approx \left( \frac{1}{n} \mathbf{J}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \right) \sqrt{n}(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0)
  \] Rearranging to solve for the estimator's distribution: \[
  \sqrt{n}(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \approx \left[ \frac{1}{n} \mathbf{J}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \right]^{-1} \left[ \frac{1}{\sqrt{n}} \mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \right]
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Convergence of Components}

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \textbf{Numerator (Score):} From the I.I.D. Score Normality theorem:
    \[
      \frac{1}{\sqrt{n}} \mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \xrightarrow{d} Z \sim N(\mathbf{0}, \mathcal{I}_1(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0))
      \]
  \item
    \textbf{Denominator (Observed Info):} By the \textbf{WLLN}, the
    average observed information converges to the expected information:
    \[
      \frac{1}{n} \mathbf{J}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) = -\frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n \nabla^2 \log f(Y_i|\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \xrightarrow{p} -E[\nabla^2 \log f] = \mathcal{I}_1(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)
      \]
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Slutsky's Theorem}

  Combining these via Slutsky's Theorem (matrix inverse is a continuous
  mapping): \[
  \sqrt{n}(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \xrightarrow{d} [\mathcal{I}_1(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)]^{-1} Z
  \] The variance of this limiting distribution is: \[
  \text{Var}([\mathcal{I}_1]^{-1} Z) = \mathcal{I}_1^{-1} \text{Var}(Z) (\mathcal{I}_1^{-1})^T = \mathcal{I}_1^{-1} \mathcal{I}_1 \mathcal{I}_1^{-1} = \mathcal{I}_1^{-1}
  \] Thus: \[
  \sqrt{n}(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_n - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \xrightarrow{d} N\left( \mathbf{0}, [\mathcal{I}_1(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)]^{-1} \right)
  \]
\end{enumerate}

\end{proof}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{mle_files/figure-pdf/fig-score-approx-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-score-approx}Linear Approximation of the Score
Function: The vertical purple segment represents the Score at the true
parameter (\(\Delta U\)), while the horizontal green segment shows the
estimation error (\(\hat{\theta}_n - \theta_0\)). The red dashed line
indicates the linear approximation (\(-J(\theta_0)\)).}

\end{figure}%

\subsection{Wilks' Theorem}\label{wilks-theorem}

\subsubsection{Wilks' Theorem (Simple Null
Hypothesis)}\label{wilks-theorem-simple-null-hypothesis}

\begin{theorem}[Wilks' Theorem (Simple Null
Hypothesis)]\protect\hypertarget{thm-wilks-simple}{}\label{thm-wilks-simple}

Consider testing \(H_0: \boldsymbol{\theta} = \boldsymbol{\theta}_0\)
against \(H_1: \boldsymbol{\theta} \ne \boldsymbol{\theta}_0\).

Define the \textbf{Likelihood Ratio} \(\Lambda_n\) as: \[
\Lambda_n = \frac{L(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)}{L(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}_{\text{MLE}})}
\]

Define the \textbf{Deviance Statistic} \(D_n\) as: \[
D_n = -2 \log \Lambda_n = 2 [\ell_n(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}) - \ell_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)]
\]

Under the null hypothesis, the Deviance converges to a Chi-squared
distribution: \[
D_n \xrightarrow{d} \chi^2_p
\] where \(p\) is the dimension of \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\).

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
We express the likelihood difference \(\Delta \ell\) in terms of the
Score vector \(\mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\) and apply the
Central Limit Theorem directly to the Score.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Quadratic Approximation of Deviance}

  Expand \(\ell_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\) around the MLE
  \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}\). Since the gradient at the MLE is zero
  (\(\mathbf{U}_n(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}) = \mathbf{0}\)), the
  first-order term vanishes: \[
   \ell_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \approx \ell_n(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}) - \frac{1}{2} (\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0)^T \mathbf{J}_n(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}) (\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0)
   \] Rearranging for the Deviance (\(D_n\)): \[
   D_n = 2[\ell_n(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}) - \ell_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)] \approx (\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0)^T \mathbf{J}_n(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}) (\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0)
   \]
\item
  \textbf{Substituting the Score}

  Recall the linear link we established in the Normality proof:
  \(\mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \approx \mathbf{J}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\).
  Inverting this relationship gives the parameter error in terms of the
  Score: \[
   (\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \approx \mathbf{J}_n^{-1} \mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)
   \] Substitute this back into the Deviance equation: \[
   D_n \approx (\mathbf{J}_n^{-1} \mathbf{U}_n)^T \mathbf{J}_n (\mathbf{J}_n^{-1} \mathbf{U}_n)
   \] \[
   \boxed{D_n \approx \mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)^T \mathbf{J}_n^{-1} \mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)}
   \] This form (the Score Statistic) shows that the deviance is
  essentially the squared length of the Score vector, standardized by
  the Information.
\item
  \textbf{Asymptotic Convergence}

  We rewrite the expression using normalized quantities to apply the
  limit theorems.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \textbf{Score:}
    \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{n}} \mathbf{U}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \xrightarrow{d} \mathbf{Z} \sim N(\mathbf{0}, \mathcal{I}_1)\).
  \item
    \textbf{Information:}
    \(\frac{1}{n} \mathbf{J}_n \xrightarrow{p} \mathcal{I}_1\).
  \end{itemize}

  \[
   D_n \approx \left( \frac{1}{\sqrt{n}} \mathbf{U}_n \right)^T \left( \frac{1}{n} \mathbf{J}_n \right)^{-1} \left( \frac{1}{\sqrt{n}} \mathbf{U}_n \right)
   \] Taking the limit: \[
   D_n \xrightarrow{d} \mathbf{Z}^T \mathcal{I}_1^{-1} \mathbf{Z}
   \] Since \(\mathbf{Z} \sim N(\mathbf{0}, \mathcal{I}_1)\), the
  quadratic form follows a Chi-squared distribution: \[
   \mathbf{Z}^T \mathcal{I}_1^{-1} \mathbf{Z} \sim \chi^2_p
   \]
\end{enumerate}

\end{proof}

\subsection{Summary of Asymptotic
Approximations}\label{summary-of-asymptotic-approximations}

\subsection{Summary of Asymptotic
Approximations}\label{summary-of-asymptotic-approximations-1}

In the limit as \(n \to \infty\) (or exactly for the Normal
distribution), the log-likelihood behaves like a quadratic function.
This leads to the following relationships between the Deviance (\(D\)),
Score (\(\mathbf{U}\)), Error (\(\Delta \boldsymbol{\theta}\)), and
Information (\(\mathcal{I}\)).

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\linewidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.3333}}@{}}
\toprule\noalign{}
\begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Relationship
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Formula
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{\linewidth}\raggedright
Intuition (Geometry of Parabola)
\end{minipage} \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
\textbf{Deviance vs.~Error} &
\(D \approx \Delta \boldsymbol{\theta}^T \mathcal{I} \Delta \boldsymbol{\theta}\)
& The vertical drop (\(D\)) is proportional to the squared horizontal
distance (\(\Delta \boldsymbol{\theta}^2\)), scaled by curvature
(\(\mathcal{I}\)). This is the \textbf{Wald Statistic}. \\
\textbf{Score vs.~Error} &
\(\mathbf{U} \approx \mathcal{I} \Delta \boldsymbol{\theta}\) & The
slope (\(\mathbf{U}\)) increases linearly with horizontal distance
(\(\Delta \boldsymbol{\theta}\)). The rate of increase is the curvature
(\(\mathcal{I}\)). \\
\textbf{Deviance vs.~Score} &
\(D \approx \mathbf{U}^T \mathcal{I}^{-1} \mathbf{U}\) & The vertical
drop (\(D\)) is proportional to the squared slope (\(\mathbf{U}^2\)),
\emph{divided} by curvature (\(\mathcal{I}\)). This is the \textbf{Score
Statistic}. \\
\textbf{Information Definition} &
\(\mathcal{I} \approx -\nabla \mathbf{U}\) & The curvature
(\(\mathcal{I}\)) is the rate of change of the slope (\(\mathbf{U}\))
(negative Hessian). \\
\textbf{Information Approximation} &
\(\mathcal{I} \approx \sum_{i=1}^n \mathbf{U}_i \mathbf{U}_i^T\) & The
curvature (\(\mathcal{I}\)) is estimated by the ``Sum of Squared
Gradients'' (Outer Products). This relies on Bartlett's 2nd Identity:
\(E[\mathcal{I}] = \text{Var}(\mathbf{U})\). \\
\end{longtable}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{mle_files/figure-pdf/fig-asymptotic-relationships-final-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-asymptotic-relationships-final}Asymptotic
Relationships: The Information matrix \(\mathcal{I}\) bridges the
spaces. Note the use of the empirical sum of outer products
\(\sum \mathbf{U}_i \mathbf{U}_i^T\) to estimate curvature.}

\end{figure}%

\section{Examples}\label{examples-4}

\subsection{Asympotic Distributions of MLE of Normal
Sample}\label{asympotic-distributions-of-mle-of-normal-sample}

\begin{example}[Example: Normal Distribution with Unknown Variance
(\(N(\mu, \sigma^2)\))]\protect\hypertarget{exm-normal-mle-unknown-variance}{}\label{exm-normal-mle-unknown-variance}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \overset{iid}{\sim} N(\mu, \sigma^2)\). The
parameter vector is \(\boldsymbol{\theta} = (\mu, \sigma^2)^T\). We
assume both parameters are unknown.

\textbf{1. Derivation of Key Quantities}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Log-Likelihood:} \[
    \ell(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = -\frac{n}{2}\log(2\pi) - \frac{n}{2}\log(\sigma^2) - \frac{1}{2\sigma^2}\sum_{i=1}^n (x_i - \mu)^2
    \]
\item
  \textbf{Score Vector \(\mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta})\):} Gradient
  w.r.t \(\mu\) and \(\sigma^2\): \[
    \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = \begin{pmatrix} \frac{\partial \ell}{\partial \mu} \\ \frac{\partial \ell}{\partial \sigma^2} \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} \frac{1}{\sigma^2}\sum (x_i - \mu) \\ -\frac{n}{2\sigma^2} + \frac{1}{2\sigma^4}\sum (x_i - \mu)^2 \end{pmatrix}
    \]
\item
  \textbf{Maximum Likelihood Estimator (\(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}\)):}
  Solving \(\mathbf{U}(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}) = \mathbf{0}\): \[
    \hat{\mu} = \bar{X}, \quad \hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n (X_i - \bar{X})^2
    \]
\item
  \textbf{Fisher Information Matrix
  \(\mathcal{I}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta})\):} The matrix of negative
  expected second derivatives (note that the cross-terms are zero,
  implying orthogonality of \(\mu\) and \(\sigma^2\)): \[
    \mathcal{I}_n(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = \begin{pmatrix} \frac{n}{\sigma^2} & 0 \\ 0 & \frac{n}{2\sigma^4} \end{pmatrix}
    \]
\end{itemize}

\textbf{2. Asymptotic Properties}

Based on the general theory, we state the asymptotic distributions for
the MLE, Score, and Deviance (testing the full vector
\(H_0: \boldsymbol{\theta} = \boldsymbol{\theta}_0\)).

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Estimator Normality:} \[
  \hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}} \overset{a}{\sim} N_2\left( \boldsymbol{\theta}, \mathcal{I}_n^{-1} \right) = N_2\left( \begin{pmatrix} \mu \\ \sigma^2 \end{pmatrix}, \begin{pmatrix} \frac{\sigma^2}{n} & 0 \\ 0 & \frac{2\sigma^4}{n} \end{pmatrix} \right)
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Score Normality:} \[
  \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta}) \overset{a}{\sim} N_2\left( \mathbf{0}, \mathcal{I}_n \right) = N_2\left( \begin{pmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \end{pmatrix}, \begin{pmatrix} \frac{n}{\sigma^2} & 0 \\ 0 & \frac{n}{2\sigma^4} \end{pmatrix} \right)
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Deviance (Wilks' Theorem):} For testing
  \(H_0: (\mu, \sigma^2) = (\mu_0, \sigma_0^2)\) vs
  \(H_1: \text{unrestricted}\): \[
  D = 2[\ell(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}) - \ell(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)] \xrightarrow{d} \chi^2_2
  \]
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{3. Analytical Verification}

We check if these asymptotic approximations match the exact
finite-sample properties.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{For the Mean (\(\mu\)):}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \textbf{Score:} \(U_\mu = \frac{n}{\sigma^2}(\bar{X} - \mu)\). Since
    \(\bar{X}\) is Normal, \(U_\mu\) is \textbf{exactly Normal} for any
    \(n\).
  \item
    \textbf{MLE:} \(\hat{\mu} = \bar{X}\) is \textbf{exactly Normal} for
    any \(n\).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{For the Variance (\(\sigma^2\)):}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \textbf{Score:}
    \(U_{\sigma^2} = \frac{1}{2\sigma^4} \left( \sum (X_i - \mu)^2 - n\sigma^2 \right)\).
    The term \(\sum (X_i - \mu)^2\) follows a scaled Chi-squared
    distribution (\(\sigma^2 \chi^2_n\)).
  \item
    \textbf{Verification:} The Score is \textbf{not exactly Normal} (it
    is a shifted Gamma/Chi-squared). However, by the Central Limit
    Theorem, as \(n \to \infty\), a Chi-squared random variable
    converges to Normality. Thus, the property holds
    \textbf{asymptotically}.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{For the Deviance (\(D\)):}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    The Deviance involves sums of squared errors and logarithms of sums
    of squares. It is \textbf{not exactly Chi-squared} for finite \(n\)
    (unlike the known-variance case). It converges to \(\chi^2_2\) as
    \(n\) increases.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\textbf{4. Simulation Verification}

We simulate 5000 datasets with \(n=100\). We calculate the standardized
MLEs, standardized Scores, and the Deviance to check convergence.

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{mle_files/figure-pdf/fig-normal-unknown-var-sim-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-normal-unknown-var-sim}Simulation of Normal (Unknown
Variance) Asymptotics (n=100). Left/Center-Left: Standardized MLEs.
Center-Right: Standardized Score for Variance. Right: Deviance (2 df).}

\end{figure}%

\end{example}

\subsection{Asymptotic Distributions of Poisson
Regression}\label{asymptotic-distributions-of-poisson-regression}

\begin{example}[Simulation Study: Poisson Regression
Asymptotics]\protect\hypertarget{exm-pr-asym}{}\label{exm-pr-asym}

In this example, we verify the asymptotic normality of the Maximum
Likelihood Estimator (MLE) and the Chi-squared distribution of the
Deviance statistic using a simulation study.

\textbf{1. Simulation Setup}

We consider a Generalized Linear Model (GLM) with a Poisson response and
a canonical log-link function: \[
Y_i \sim \text{Poisson}(\lambda_i), \quad \log(\lambda_i) = \beta_0 + \beta_1 x_i
\]

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{True Parameters:} We set the true coefficient vector to
  \(\boldsymbol{\beta} = (0.5, 1.5)^\top\).
\item
  \textbf{Covariates:} We generate \(n=200\) covariate values \(x_i\)
  from a Uniform(0, 1) distribution. These remain fixed across all
  simulations.
\item
  \textbf{Data Generation:} For each simulation iteration, we generate
  response vector \(\mathbf{y}\) using the true mean
  \(\lambda_i = \exp(0.5 + 1.5 x_i)\).
\end{itemize}

\textbf{2. Model Fitting and Statistics}

For each of the \texttt{n\_sim\ =\ 2000} generated datasets, we perform
the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Fit the Model:} We estimate \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}}\) using
  the \texttt{glm()} function in R.
\item
  \textbf{Calculate Standardized MLEs (Wald Statistic):} \[
  Z_{\hat{\beta}_j} = \frac{\hat{\beta}_j - \beta_{j, \text{true}}}{\text{SE}(\hat{\beta}_j)}
  \] The standard errors are derived from the inverse of the Fisher
  Information matrix,
  \(\mathcal{I}^{-1} = (\mathbf{X}^\top \mathbf{W} \mathbf{X})^{-1}\).
\item
  \textbf{Calculate Standardized Score:} \[
  Z_{U_j} = \frac{U_j(\boldsymbol{\beta}_{\text{true}})}{\sqrt{\text{Var}(U_j)}}
  \] The score is evaluated at the \emph{true} parameter values.
\item
  \textbf{Calculate Deviance:} \[
  D = 2 \left( \ell(\hat{\boldsymbol{\beta}}) - \ell(\boldsymbol{\beta}_{\text{true}}) \right)
  \] This statistic compares the fitted model to the true model.
  According to Wilks' theorem, this should follow a \(\chi^2_p\)
  distribution (where \(p=2\) is the number of parameters).
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{3. Simulation Code and Results}

The histograms below overlay the theoretical asymptotic densities
(Red/Blue lines) on top of the empirical histograms. The close alignment
confirms that even for a moderate sample size of \(n=200\), the
asymptotic approximations are highly accurate.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
