\section{MLE and Penalized Likelihood in Deep
Learning}\label{mle-and-penalized-likelihood-in-deep-learning}

In deep learning, the objective function is typically the negative
log-likelihood of the dataset, often supplemented by a penalty term
(regularization) to prevent overfitting. While the theoretical
foundations of Maximum Likelihood Estimation remain the same, the
practical application in high-dimensional neural networks faces
significant computational challenges that necessitate specialized
optimization strategies.

\subsection{The Optimization
Challenge}\label{the-optimization-challenge}

In traditional statistical models, \(\ell(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) is
often globally concave, allowing algorithms like Newton-Raphson to
converge reliably. Deep learning models, however, present a different
landscape:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Non-convexity} \n

  The log-likelihood surface \(\ell(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) is highly
  non-convex with numerous local minima, saddle points, and plateaus.
\item
  \textbf{High Dimensionality} \n

  The parameter vector \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\) can contain millions or
  billions of weights (\(p \gg 10^6\)). This makes the storage and
  inversion of the Observed Information Matrix
  \(\mathbf{J}(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) (the \(p \times p\) Hessian)
  computationally intractable, as its storage requires \(O(p^2)\)
  memory.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Penalized Likelihood
(Regularization)}\label{penalized-likelihood-regularization}

To improve generalization, we often optimize a \textbf{penalized
log-likelihood}:

\[
\ell_{pen}(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = \ell(\boldsymbol{\beta}) - \Omega(\boldsymbol{\theta})
\]

where \(\Omega(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) is a penalty function. Common
examples include:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{\(L_2\) Regularization (Weight Decay):}
  \(\Omega(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = \frac{\lambda}{2} \|\boldsymbol{\theta}\|^2\),
  which corresponds to a Gaussian prior in a Bayesian framework.
\item
  \textbf{\(L_1\) Regularization (Lasso):}
  \(\Omega(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = \lambda \|\boldsymbol{\theta}\|_1\),
  which encourages sparsity in the network weights.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Purely Gradient-Based Methods (O(p)
Complexity)}\label{purely-gradient-based-methods-op-complexity}

Because the Hessian is too large to compute, the most common approach is
to use methods that only require the Score vector
\(\mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\theta})\). These methods have a memory
complexity of \(O(p)\), making them feasible for large-scale problems.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD)} \n

  Instead of the full score, SGD uses a ``mini-batch'' estimate
  \(\mathbf{U}_{batch}\). The update is: \[
  \boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t+1)} = \boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)} + \alpha \mathbf{U}_{batch}(\boldsymbol{\theta}^{(t)})
  \] While simple, SGD treats all dimensions equally, which is
  inefficient if the likelihood surface is much steeper in some
  directions than others.
\item
  \textbf{Momentum-Based Methods} \n

  These maintain a moving average of past scores to dampen oscillations
  and accelerate progress in consistent directions, effectively acting
  as a low-pass filter on the noisy stochastic gradients.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Avoiding the Hessian via Score
Covariance}\label{avoiding-the-hessian-via-score-covariance}

Modern optimizers leverage the \textbf{Second Moment Identity}
(Bartlett's Identity) to capture curvature information without the
\(O(p^2)\) cost of the Hessian.

\begin{theorem}[Fisher Information Approximation and the Empirical
Fisher]\protect\hypertarget{thm-fisher-approximation}{}\label{thm-fisher-approximation}

In the context of high-dimensional optimization, the identity
\(\mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\theta}) = \text{Cov}_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0}(\mathbf{U}) = E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0}[\mathbf{J}]\)
is not just a theoretical curiosity, but a computational lifeline.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Justification via the Second Moment} \n

  Recall that for the score vector \(\mathbf{U}\), we have
  \(E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0}[\mathbf{U}] = \mathbf{0}\). Therefore, the
  covariance matrix simplifies to the second moment:

  \[
  \text{Cov}(\mathbf{U}) = E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0}[\mathbf{U}\mathbf{U}^\top] - E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0}[\mathbf{U}]E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0}[\mathbf{U}]^\top = E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0}[\mathbf{U}\mathbf{U}^\top]
  \]

  Under Bartlett's Second Identity, this expectation is exactly equal to
  the expected Observed Information (the expected negative Hessian),
  \(E_{\boldsymbol{\theta}_0}[\mathbf{J}]\).
\item
  \textbf{The Empirical Fisher Estimate} \n

  In deep learning, we cannot compute the expectation over all possible
  data. Instead, we use the \textbf{Empirical Fisher Information Matrix}
  (\(\hat{\mathcal{I}}\)), which replaces the population expectation
  with the sample average across \(n\) observations:

  \[
  \hat{\mathcal{I}} = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n \left( \nabla_{\boldsymbol{\theta}} \log f(y_i | \boldsymbol{\theta}) \right) \left( \nabla_{\boldsymbol{\theta}} \log f(y_i | \boldsymbol{\theta}) \right)^\top
  \]

  This estimate is constructed solely from \textbf{first-order
  gradients} (the score for each data point).
\item
  \textbf{Handling High Dimensionality (\(p\))} \n

  Since \(\hat{\mathcal{I}}\) is a \(p \times p\) matrix, storing it is
  \(O(p^2)\). For a model with 1 million parameters, the matrix would
  have \(10^{12}\) elements. To handle this, deep learning optimizers
  typically use the \textbf{Diagonal Empirical Fisher}:

  \[
  \text{diag}(\hat{\mathcal{I}}) = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^n [ \mathbf{U}_i \odot \mathbf{U}_i ]
  \]

  where \(\odot\) is the Hadamard (element-wise) product. This reduces
  the complexity to \(O(p)\).
\item
  \textbf{Why use the Score Covariance instead of the Hessian?} \n

  Computing the diagonal of the Hessian \(\mathbf{J}\) directly (the
  second derivatives \(\frac{\partial^2 \ell}{\partial \theta_j^2}\)) is
  significantly more expensive than computing the gradient. Most deep
  learning frameworks are optimized for ``backpropagation'' (first
  derivatives). By using the squared gradients to approximate the
  diagonal of the Hessian, we get ``second-order-like'' curvature
  information for the price of first-order computation.
\end{enumerate}

\end{theorem}

\begin{refremark}[A Note on the Approximation]
While \(\text{Cov}(\mathbf{U}) = E[\mathbf{J}]\) holds at the true
parameter \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\), it may not hold perfectly during
the early stages of training when \(\boldsymbol{\theta}\) is far from
the truth. In these regions, the empirical Fisher is technically an
approximation of the \emph{variability} of the gradients, which often
serves as a robust proxy for the \emph{sensitivity} (curvature) of the
loss surface.

\label{rem-empirical-fisher-caveat}

\end{refremark}

\subsection{Adaptive Methods: Diagonal Covariance
Scaling}\label{adaptive-methods-diagonal-covariance-scaling}

Algorithms like \textbf{RMSProp} and \textbf{Adam} avoid the
\(p \times p\) matrix entirely by assuming the Fisher Information is
approximately diagonal. This reduces the memory cost back to \(O(p)\).

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{RMSProp} \n

  It maintains a running average of the squared scores for each
  parameter \(j\): \[
  v_j^{(t)} = \gamma v_j^{(t-1)} + (1-\gamma) (U_j^{(t)})^2
  \] Since \((U_j)^2\) is a stochastic estimate of the variance
  \(\text{Var}(U_j) = \mathcal{I}_{jj}\), the term \(\sqrt{v_j}\)
  approximates the local curvature (sensitivity) along that coordinate.
\item
  \textbf{Adam (Adaptive Moment Estimation)} \n

  Adam combines the concepts of momentum and RMSProp by maintaining
  moving averages of both the first and second moments of the score
  vector. It scales the update by the inverse square root of the
  estimated variance:

  \[
  \theta_j^{(t+1)} = \theta_j^{(t)} + \alpha \frac{\hat{m}_j}{\sqrt{\hat{v}_j} + \epsilon}
  \]

  Here, the terms \(m_j\) and \(v_j\) are defined as:

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \textbf{The First Moment (\(m_j\)):} An exponentially decaying
    average of the score \(U_j\), which acts as momentum to smooth out
    noisy gradients: \[
    m_j^{(t)} = \beta_1 m_j^{(t-1)} + (1 - \beta_1) U_j^{(t)}
    \]
  \item
    \textbf{The Second Moment (\(v_j\)):} An exponentially decaying
    average of the squared score \((U_j)^2\): \[
    v_j^{(t)} = \beta_2 v_j^{(t-1)} + (1 - \beta_2) (U_j^{(t)})^2
    \] Following the logic of Theorem~\ref{thm-fisher-approximation},
    \(v_j\) serves as an estimate of the diagonal of the Fisher
    Information Matrix (\(\mathcal{I}_{jj}\)).
  \item
    \textbf{Bias Correction:} Because \(m\) and \(v\) are typically
    initialized at zero, they are biased toward zero during the initial
    steps. Adam uses the bias-corrected estimates
    \(\hat{m}_j = m_j / (1 - \beta_1^t)\) and
    \(\hat{v}_j = v_j / (1 - \beta_2^t)\) for the final update.
  \end{itemize}

  This ``preconditioning'' flattens the likelihood surface by
  normalizing the step size for each parameter. Dimensions with high
  gradient variance (high \(\mathcal{I}_{jj}\)) receive smaller updates,
  while dimensions with low variance receive larger updates, effectively
  performing a coordinate-wise Newton-Raphson step using the Score
  Covariance as a proxy for the Hessian.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{refremark}[Adam as Quasi-Newton]
From a statistical perspective, Adam can be viewed as an approximation
of the Fisher Scoring algorithm. Instead of calculating the full
expected information matrix and its inverse, Adam uses the moving
average of squared gradients to estimate the diagonal entries of the
information matrix in an online fashion. This allows the algorithm to
adapt to the local geometry of the log-likelihood surface at a minimal
\(O(p)\) computational cost.

\label{rem-adam-interpretation}

\end{refremark}

\begin{refremark}[Natural Gradient and K-FAC]
The most sophisticated application of this theory is \textbf{Natural
Gradient Descent}. To handle large \(p\), methods like \textbf{K-FAC
(Kronecker-factored Approximate Curvature)} approximate the Fisher
Information Matrix as a product of smaller matrices. This allows for a
more ``Hessian-like'' update that accounts for correlations between
parameters while remaining computationally efficient.

\label{rem-natural-gradient}

\end{refremark}

\section{Appendix: Derivation of Score and Information in Poisson
Regression}\label{appendix-derivation-of-score-and-information-in-poisson-regression}

\begin{example}[]\protect\hypertarget{exm-poisson-score-fisher}{}\label{exm-poisson-score-fisher}

In a Poisson Generalized Linear Model with a canonical link, the mean is
modeled as
\(\lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \exp(\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta})\).
The derivation of the Score vector and the Fisher Information matrix
relies on the chain rule and the properties of the exponential function.

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Derivation of the Score Vector
\(\mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\beta})\)}{Derivation of the Score Vector \textbackslash mathbf\{U\}(\textbackslash boldsymbol\{\textbackslash beta\})}}\label{derivation-of-the-score-vector-mathbfuboldsymbolbeta}

The Score vector is defined as the gradient of the log-likelihood with
respect to the parameter vector \(\boldsymbol{\beta}\).

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Differentiate the Log-Likelihood} \n

  Starting from the log-likelihood function: \[
  \ell(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \sum_{i=1}^n \left( y_i (\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta}) - \exp(\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta}) - \log(y_i!) \right)
  \] We take the partial derivative with respect to a single component
  \(\beta_j\): \[
  \frac{\partial \ell}{\partial \beta_j} = \sum_{i=1}^n \left( y_i \frac{\partial (\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta})}{\partial \beta_j} - \frac{\partial \exp(\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta})}{\partial \beta_j} \right)
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Apply the Chain Rule} \n

  Note that
  \(\frac{\partial (\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta})}{\partial \beta_j} = x_{ij}\).
  Using the chain rule on the exponential term: \[
  \frac{\partial \exp(\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta})}{\partial \beta_j} = \exp(\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta}) \cdot x_{ij} = \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta}) x_{ij}
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Form the Vector} \n

  Substituting these back, we get: \[
  \frac{\partial \ell}{\partial \beta_j} = \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta})) x_{ij}
  \] In matrix notation, this corresponds to: \[
  \mathbf{U}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \mathbf{X}^\top (\mathbf{y} - \boldsymbol{\lambda}(\boldsymbol{\beta}))
  \]
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Derivation of the Observed Fisher
Information
\(J(\boldsymbol{\beta})\)}{Derivation of the Observed Fisher Information J(\textbackslash boldsymbol\{\textbackslash beta\})}}\label{derivation-of-the-observed-fisher-information-jboldsymbolbeta}

The Observed Information is the negative Hessian matrix of the
log-likelihood.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Differentiate the Score} \n

  We take the derivative of the \(j\)-th component of the score,
  \(U_j = \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta})) x_{ij}\),
  with respect to \(\beta_k\): \[
  \frac{\partial^2 \ell}{\partial \beta_j \partial \beta_k} = \frac{\partial}{\partial \beta_k} \sum_{i=1}^n (y_i - \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta})) x_{ij} = \sum_{i=1}^n \left( -\frac{\partial \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta})}{\partial \beta_k} \right) x_{ij}
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Differentiate the Mean Function} \n

  Recall
  \(\lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \exp(\mathbf{x}_i^\top \boldsymbol{\beta})\).
  Its derivative with respect to \(\beta_k\) is: \[
  \frac{\partial \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta})}{\partial \beta_k} = \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta}) x_{ik}
  \]
\item
  \textbf{Construct the Information Matrix} \n

  The \((j, k)\) element of the Hessian is: \[
  H_{jk} = -\sum_{i=1}^n \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta}) x_{ij} x_{ik}
  \] The Observed Information \(J(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = -H\) is
  therefore: \[
  J(\boldsymbol{\beta})_{jk} = \sum_{i=1}^n \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta}) x_{ij} x_{ik}
  \] In matrix form, let \(\mathbf{W}(\boldsymbol{\beta})\) be a
  diagonal matrix where \(W_{ii} = \lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta})\).
  Then: \[
  J(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \mathbf{X}^\top \mathbf{W}(\boldsymbol{\beta}) \mathbf{X}
  \]
\end{enumerate}

\begin{refremark}[Canonical Link Advantage]
Notice that because we used the canonical link
(\(\log \lambda = \eta\)), the second derivative of the log-likelihood
(the Hessian) does not depend on the observed data \(y_i\). It only
depends on the predicted means \(\lambda_i(\boldsymbol{\beta})\).
Consequently, the \textbf{Observed Information} and the \textbf{Expected
Information} are identical: \[
J(\boldsymbol{\beta}) = \mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\beta})
\] This is a unique property of canonical links in Generalized Linear
Models.

\label{rem-canonical-simplicity}

\end{refremark}

\end{example}

\bookmarksetup{startatroot}

\chapter{Minimum Variance Estimators}\label{minimum-variance-estimators}

\section{Completeness}\label{completeness}

\subsection{Complete Statistic}\label{complete-statistic}

\begin{definition}[Complete
Statistic]\protect\hypertarget{def-completeness}{}\label{def-completeness}

A statistic \(T\) is said to be \textbf{complete} if for any real-valued
function \(g\), \[E[g(T)|\theta] = 0 \quad \text{for all } \theta\]
implies \[P(g(T) = 0 | \theta) = 1 \quad \text{for all } \theta\]

\end{definition}

Significance: If \(T\) is complete, then there exists at most one
unbiased estimator for \(\theta\) that is a function of \(T\).

\begin{example}[Uniform Distribution (Not
Complete)]\protect\hypertarget{exm-uniform-incomplete}{}\label{exm-uniform-incomplete}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \sim \text{Unif}(\theta-1, \theta+1)\). The
statistic \(T(X) = (X_{(1)}, X_{(n)})\) is a Minimal Sufficient
Statistic. However, it is \textbf{not complete}.

Consider the range \(R = X_{(n)} - X_{(1)}\). The distribution of \(R\)
does not depend on \(\theta\) (it is an ancillary statistic). Let
\(g(T) = X_{(n)} - X_{(1)} - c\), where \(c = E[X_{(n)} - X_{(1)}]\).
Then \(E[g(T)] = 0\) for all \(\theta\), but \(g(T)\) is not identically
zero.

\end{example}

\subsection{Exponential Family
Completeness}\label{exponential-family-completeness}

\begin{lemma}[Exponential Family
Completeness]\protect\hypertarget{lem-exponential-complete}{}\label{lem-exponential-complete}

If \(T = (T_1, \dots, T_k)\) is the natural statistic of an exponential
family that contains an open rectangle in the parameter space, then
\(T\) is complete.

\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
\textbf{Proof of Lemma Lemma~\ref{lem-exponential-complete}:}

Consider a \(k\)-parameter exponential family in canonical form with
density:
\[f(y|\boldsymbol{\eta}) = h(y) \exp\left( \sum_{j=1}^k \eta_j T_j(y) - A(\boldsymbol{\eta}) \right)\]
where \(\boldsymbol{\eta}\) belongs to a natural parameter space \(\Xi\)
that contains a \(k\)-dimensional open rectangle.

Let \(g(T)\) be a function such that \(E_{\boldsymbol{\eta}}[g(T)] = 0\)
for all \(\boldsymbol{\eta} \in \Xi\). The expectation is defined as:
\[\int g(t) h(t) \exp\left( \sum_{j=1}^k \eta_j t_j - A(\boldsymbol{\eta}) \right) dt = 0\]

Since \(\exp(-A(\boldsymbol{\eta}))\) is a strictly positive constant
with respect to \(t\), we can divide it out:
\[\int g(t) h(t) \exp\left( \sum_{j=1}^k \eta_j t_j \right) dt = 0\]

We can decompose \(g(t)\) into its positive and negative parts,
\(g(t) = g^+(t) - g^-(t)\). Substituting this in gives:
\[\int g^+(t) h(t) e^{\sum \eta_j t_j} dt = \int g^-(t) h(t) e^{\sum \eta_j t_j} dt\]

These integrals represent the \textbf{multivariate Laplace transforms}
of the measures \(m^+(t) = g^+(t)h(t)\) and \(m^-(t) = g^-(t)h(t)\). A
fundamental property of Laplace transforms is that if two transforms are
equal over an open set (in this case, the open rectangle in \(\Xi\)),
then the underlying measures must be identical almost everywhere.

Therefore, \(g^+(t)h(t) = g^-(t)h(t)\), which implies \(g(t) = 0\)
almost everywhere with respect to the distribution of \(T\). Thus, \(T\)
is complete.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Relationship Between Completeness and
Minimality}\label{relationship-between-completeness-and-minimality}

While sufficiency ensures that a statistic captures all information
about \(\theta\), and minimality ensures it does so without redundancy,
completeness is a stronger property that guarantees the statistic is
``uniquely informative'' for unbiased estimation.

\begin{theorem}[Completeness to Minimal
Sufficiency]\protect\hypertarget{thm-complete-minimal}{}\label{thm-complete-minimal}

If \(T\) is a \textbf{complete sufficient statistic} for a family of
distributions \(\{f(x|\theta) : \theta \in \Theta\}\), then \(T\) is
also a \textbf{minimal sufficient statistic}.

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
To prove \(T\) is minimal sufficient, we must show that for any other
sufficient statistic \(S\), \(T\) is a function of \(S\).

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Define a Conditional Expectation:}

  Let \(S\) be any sufficient statistic. Define the statistic
  \(\phi(S) = E[T|S]\). Because \(S\) is sufficient, \(\phi(S)\) is a
  valid statistic (it does not depend on \(\theta\)).
\item
  \textbf{Compare Expectations:}

  By the Law of Iterated Expectations: \[E[\phi(S)] = E[E[T|S]] = E[T]\]
  This implies that \(E[\phi(S) - T] = 0\) for all
  \(\theta \in \Theta\).
\item
  \textbf{Construct a Function of T:}

  Define \(h(T) = E[\phi(S)|T]\). Since \(T\) is sufficient, \(h(T)\) is
  a statistic. By the properties of expectation:
  \[E[h(T)] = E[E[\phi(S)|T]] = E[\phi(S)] = E[T]\] Therefore,
  \(E[h(T) - T] = 0\) for all \(\theta \in \Theta\).
\item
  \textbf{Apply Completeness:}

  Since \(g(T) = h(T) - T\) is a function of \(T\) whose expectation is
  zero for all \(\theta\), and \(T\) is \textbf{complete}, it follows
  that: \[g(T) = 0 \implies h(T) = T \quad \text{with probability 1.}\]
\item
  \textbf{Conclusion:}

  Since \(T\) is a function of \(\phi(S)\) (which is a function of
  \(S\)), \(T\) must be a function of \(S\). Since this holds for any
  sufficient statistic \(S\), \(T\) is minimal sufficient.
\end{enumerate}

\end{proof}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{umvue_files/figure-pdf/fig-proof-diagram-r-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-proof-diagram-r}Logical flow of the proof: Showing T
is a function of any sufficient statistic S.}

\end{figure}%

\begin{remark}
It is important to note that the converse is not generally true. A
\textbf{minimal sufficient statistic is not necessarily complete}. A
standard counterexample is the \(\text{Unif}(\theta-1, \theta+1)\)
distribution, where the minimal sufficient statistic
\((X_{(1)}, X_{(n)})\) is not complete because the range
\(X_{(n)} - X_{(1)}\) is an ancillary statistic.
\end{remark}

The relationship between statistics types is visualized by
Figure~\ref{fig-stats-hierarchy}:

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{umvue_files/figure-pdf/fig-stats-hierarchy-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-stats-hierarchy}Hierarchy of Statistics:
Completeness implies Minimal Sufficiency under regularity.}

\end{figure}%

\section{Uniformly Minimum Variance Unbiased Estimator
(UMVUE)}\label{uniformly-minimum-variance-unbiased-estimator-umvue}

\subsection{Definition}\label{definition}

\begin{definition}[Uniformly Minimum Variance Unbiased Estimator
(UMVUE)]\protect\hypertarget{def-umvue}{}\label{def-umvue}

A statistic \(T(x)\) is a UMVUE for \(\theta\) if:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \(E(T(x)|\theta) = \theta\) for all \(\theta\) (Unbiased).
\item
  \(\text{Var}(T(x)|\theta) \le \text{Var}(d(x)|\theta)\) for all
  \(\theta\) and for all other unbiased estimators \(d(x)\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{definition}

\subsection{Rao-Blackwell Theorem}\label{rao-blackwell-theorem}

The Rao-Blackwell theorem provides a mechanism for improving an
estimator by utilizing a sufficient statistic.

\begin{theorem}[Rao-Blackwell
Theorem]\protect\hypertarget{thm-rao-blackwell}{}\label{thm-rao-blackwell}

Given that \(T\) is a sufficient statistic and \(d_1(x)\) is an unbiased
estimator (\(E[d_1(x)] = \theta\)). Define \(g(T) = E[d_1(x) | T]\).
Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \(g(T)\) is a statistic (independent of \(\theta\)).
\item
  \(E[g(T)] = \theta\) (Unbiased).
\item
  \(\text{Var}(g(T)) \le \text{Var}(d_1(x))\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\leavevmode

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Statistic Property:} By the definition of sufficiency, the
  conditional distribution of \(X\) given \(T\) does not depend on
  \(\theta\). Thus, \(g(T) = E[d_1(x)|T]\) does not involve \(\theta\)
  and is a valid statistic.
\item
  \textbf{Unbiasedness:} By the Law of Iterated Expectations:

  \[E[g(T)] = E_T[ E_X(d_1(X)|T) ] = E_X[d_1(X)] = \theta\]
\item
  \textbf{Variance Reduction:} Using the Law of Total Variance:

  \[\text{Var}(d_1(X)) = \text{Var}(E[d_1(X)|T]) + E[\text{Var}(d_1(X)|T)] = \text{Var}(g(T)) + E[\text{Var}(d_1(X)|T)]\]
  Since \(\text{Var}(d_1(X)|T) \ge 0\), it follows that
  \(E[\text{Var}(d_1(X)|T)] \ge 0\). Therefore,
  \(\text{Var}(g(T)) \le \text{Var}(d_1(X))\). Equality holds only if
  \(d_1(X) = g(T)\) almost surely.
\end{enumerate}

\end{proof}

\subsection{Lehmann-Scheffé Theorem}\label{lehmann-scheffuxe9-theorem}

While Rao-Blackwell improves an estimator, Lehmann-Scheffé ensures that
we have found the \emph{best} one.

\begin{theorem}[Lehmann-Scheffé
Theorem]\protect\hypertarget{thm-lehmann-scheffe}{}\label{thm-lehmann-scheffe}

If \(T\) is a complete and sufficient statistic, and there is an
unbiased estimator \(d(X)\) such that \(E[d(X)] = \theta\), then
\(\phi(T) = E[d(X)|T]\) is the unique UMVUE for \(\theta\).

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\leavevmode

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Existence:} From Rao-Blackwell, \(\phi(T)\) is an unbiased
  estimator with variance at most that of any \(d(X)\).
\item
  \textbf{Uniqueness:} Suppose there exists another unbiased estimator
  \(\psi(T)\) that is also a function of the same complete sufficient
  statistic \(T\). Then:

  \[E[\phi(T) - \psi(T) | \theta] = E[\phi(T)|\theta] - E[\psi(T)|\theta] = \theta - \theta = 0\]
  By the property of \textbf{completeness} for \(T\), \(E[g(T)] = 0\)
  implies \(P(g(T)=0) = 1\). Thus, \(\phi(T) = \psi(T)\) almost surely.
  This proves that \(\phi(T)\) is the unique unbiased estimator based on
  \(T\), and therefore the unique UMVUE.
\end{enumerate}

\end{proof}

\section{Connection to Likelihood
Theory}\label{connection-to-likelihood-theory}

In the previous chapters, we defined the \textbf{Score function}
\(U(\theta) = \frac{\partial}{\partial \theta} \ell(\theta)\). By the
\textbf{Fisher-Neyman Factorization Theorem}, if \(T\) is sufficient,
then \(U(\theta)\) is a function of \(T\):
\[\ell(\theta) = \log h(x) + \log g(T(x), \theta) \implies U(\theta) = \frac{\partial}{\partial \theta} \log g(T(x), \theta)\]
This confirms that the curvature and gradients of the log-likelihood are
entirely contained within the sufficient statistic \(T\), explaining why
the UMVUE must be a function of \(T\).

\section{Methods for Finding UMVUE}\label{methods-for-finding-umvue}

To find the UMVUE for a parameter \(\theta\):

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Find a Complete Sufficient Statistic:} Identify \(T\) (usually
  via Exponential Family natural statistics).
\item
  \textbf{Find an Unbiased Estimator:} Find any simple \(d(X)\) such
  that \(E[d(X)] = \theta\).
\item
  \textbf{Rao-Blackwellize:} Compute \(g(T) = E[d(X)|T]\).
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Example: Poisson UMVUE for
\(\lambda^2\)}{Example: Poisson UMVUE for \textbackslash lambda\^{}2}}\label{example-poisson-umvue-for-lambda2}

\begin{example}[Poisson UMVUE for
\(\lambda^2\)]\protect\hypertarget{exm-poisson-umvue}{}\label{exm-poisson-umvue}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \sim \text{Poisson}(\lambda)\). \(T = \sum X_i\)
is complete sufficient.
\(E[X_1^2 - X_1] = E[X_1^2] - E[X_1] = (\lambda^2 + \lambda) - \lambda = \lambda^2\).
So \(d(X) = X_1^2 - X_1\) is unbiased. The UMVUE is
\(g(T) = E[X_1(X_1-1) | T]\). Using the moments of
\(X_1|T \sim \text{Bin}(T, 1/n)\): \[g(T) = \frac{T(T-1)}{n^2}\]

\end{example}

\subsection{Example: Joint UMVUE in the Normal
Family}\label{example-joint-umvue-in-the-normal-family}

The power of the Exponential Family lemma is most evident when dealing
with distributions involving multiple parameters, such as the Normal
distribution.

\begin{example}[Joint UMVUE for \(\mu\) and
\(\sigma^2\)]\protect\hypertarget{exm-normal-joint-umvue}{}\label{exm-normal-joint-umvue}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \sim N(\mu, \sigma^2)\) where both parameters are
unknown. The joint density can be written in the canonical exponential
form:

\[f(\mathbf{x}|\mu, \sigma^2) = \underbrace{(2\pi\sigma^2)^{-n/2} \exp\left(-\frac{n\mu^2}{2\sigma^2}\right)}_{C(\boldsymbol{\eta})} \exp\left( \frac{\mu}{\sigma^2}\sum x_i - \frac{1}{2\sigma^2}\sum x_i^2 \right)\]

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Identify Natural Statistics:}

  The natural sufficient statistics are \(T_1 = \sum X_i\) and
  \(T_2 = \sum X_i^2\). Because the natural parameter space contains an
  open rectangle in \(\mathbb{R} \times \mathbb{R}^-\), the vector
  \((T_1, T_2)\) is \textbf{complete and sufficient}.
\item
  \textbf{Transformation to Common Estimators:}

  Any one-to-one mapping of a complete sufficient statistic is also
  complete and sufficient. We can define:
  \[\bar{X} = \frac{T_1}{n} \quad \text{and} \quad S^2 = \frac{1}{n-1}\left( T_2 - \frac{T_1^2}{n} \right)\]
  Since \((\bar{X}, S^2)\) is a one-to-one function of \((T_1, T_2)\),
  it is also a \textbf{complete sufficient statistic}.
\item
  \textbf{Applying Lehmann-Scheffé:}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Since \(E[\bar{X}] = \mu\) and \(\bar{X}\) is a function of the
    complete sufficient statistic, \(\bar{X}\) is the \textbf{UMVUE for
    \(\mu\)}.
  \item
    Since \(E[S^2] = \sigma^2\) and \(S^2\) is a function of the
    complete sufficient statistic, \(S^2\) is the \textbf{UMVUE for
    \(\sigma^2\)}.
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

\end{example}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{UMVUE for \(\log(\sigma^2)\) in the Normal
Family}{UMVUE for \textbackslash log(\textbackslash sigma\^{}2) in the Normal Family}}\label{umvue-for-logsigma2-in-the-normal-family}

\begin{example}[UMVUE for \(\log(\sigma^2)\) in the Normal
Family]\protect\hypertarget{exm-normal-log-variance}{}\label{exm-normal-log-variance}

While we saw that no UMVUE exists for \(\log(\lambda)\) in the Poisson
case, an unbiased estimator for the log-variance \emph{does} exist in
the Normal distribution \(N(\mu, \sigma^2)\).

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{The Target:} We want to estimate
  \(g(\sigma^2) = \log(\sigma^2)\).
\item
  \textbf{Distributional Fact:} Recall that for a sample of size \(n\),
  the statistic \(Y = \frac{(n-1)S^2}{\sigma^2}\) follows a
  \(\chi^2_{n-1}\) distribution (which is a
  \(\text{Gamma}(\alpha = \frac{n-1}{2}, \beta = 2)\) distribution).
\item
  \textbf{Log-Moments of Gamma:} For a variable
  \(W \sim \text{Gamma}(\alpha, \beta)\), the expected value of its
  logarithm is:

  \[E[\log(W)] = \psi(\alpha) + \log(\beta)\] where
  \(\psi(x) = \frac{d}{dx} \log \Gamma(x)\) is the \textbf{digamma
  function}.
\item
  \textbf{Deriving the Estimator:}

  Expanding the expectation of \(Y\) and rearranging to isolate
  \(\log(\sigma^2)\):
  \[E\left[ \log(S^2) + \left( \log\left(\frac{n-1}{2}\right) - \psi\left(\frac{n-1}{2}\right) \right) \right] = \log(\sigma^2)\]
\item
  \textbf{Conclusion:}

  Since \(S^2\) is a complete sufficient statistic, the
  \textbf{Lehmann-Scheffé Theorem} implies:
  \[\widehat{\log(\sigma^2)}_{\text{UMVUE}} = \log(S^2) + C(n)\] where
  \(C(n) = \log\left(\frac{n-1}{2}\right) - \psi\left(\frac{n-1}{2}\right)\)
  is the \textbf{positive} correction term.
\end{enumerate}

Because the logarithm is a concave function, Jensen's Inequality tells
us that \(E[\log(S^2)] < \log(E[S^2]) = \log(\sigma^2)\). Therefore,
\(\log(S^2)\) is ``too small,'' and we must add a positive value
\(C(n)\) to reach unbiasedness.

\textbf{Numerical Values of the Correction}

As seen in Table~\ref{tbl-log-bias-correction}, the bias is quite severe
for small samples but vanishes as \(n \to \infty\).

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ccc@{}}

\caption{\label{tbl-log-bias-correction}Positive correction factors
\(C(n)\) to be added to \(\log(S^2)\)}

\tabularnewline

\toprule\noalign{}
\(n\) & \(\alpha = \frac{n-1}{2}\) & Positive Correction \(C(n)\) \\
\midrule\noalign{}
\endhead
\bottomrule\noalign{}
\endlastfoot
2 & 0.5 & 1.2704 \\
5 & 2.0 & 0.2704 \\
10 & 4.5 & 0.1152 \\
30 & 14.5 & 0.0349 \\
100 & 49.5 & 0.0101 \\

\end{longtable}

\end{example}

\bookmarksetup{startatroot}

\chapter{Likelihood-based Hypothesis
Testing}\label{likelihood-based-hypothesis-testing}

\section{Formulation for Hypothesis
Testing}\label{formulation-for-hypothesis-testing}

\subsection{Hypothesis}\label{hypothesis}

We formulate the problem of hypothesis testing as deciding between two
competing claims about a parameter \(\theta\):

\[
H_0: \theta \in \Theta_0 \quad \text{(Null Hypothesis)}
\]

\[
H_1: \theta \in \Theta_1 \quad \text{(Alternative Hypothesis)}
\]

\begin{definition}[Simple and Composite
Hypotheses]\protect\hypertarget{def-simple-composite}{}\label{def-simple-composite}

A hypothesis is called \textbf{simple} if it specifies a single value
for the parameter (e.g., \(\Theta_0\) contains only one point). It is
called \textbf{composite} if it specifies more than one value.

\end{definition}

\begin{example}[Normal Mean
Test]\protect\hypertarget{exm-normal-hypotheses}{}\label{exm-normal-hypotheses}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \overset{iid}{\sim} N(\mu, \sigma^2)\).

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  If \(\sigma^2\) is known, \(H_0: \mu = \mu_0\) is a simple hypothesis.
\item
  If \(\sigma^2\) is unknown, \(H_0: \mu = \mu_0\) is a composite
  hypothesis (since \(\sigma^2\) can vary).
\end{itemize}

\end{example}

\subsection{Test Functions}\label{test-functions}

A test is defined by a \textbf{critical region} \(C_\alpha\) such that
we reject \(H_0\) if the data \(x \in C_\alpha\). Equivalently, we can
define a \textbf{test function} \(\phi(x)\) representing the probability
of rejecting \(H_0\) given data \(x\).

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A \textbf{non-randomized} test is given as follows:
\end{itemize}

\[
\phi(x) = I(x \in C_\alpha) = \begin{cases} 1 & \text{if } x \in C_\alpha \text{ (Reject } H_0 \text{)} \\ 0 & \text{otherwise} \end{cases}
\]

\begin{itemize}
\item
  A \textbf{randomized} test, \(\phi(x)\) can take values in \([0, 1]\),
  which can be expressed typically as follows:

  \[
   \phi(x) = \begin{cases} 
   1 & \text{if } x \in C_1 \\
   \gamma & \text{if } x \in C_* \\
   0 & \text{otherwise}
   \end{cases}
   \]

  where:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(C_1\) is the region where we strictly reject \(H_0\).
  \item
    \(C_*\) is the boundary region (often where \(T(x) = k\)) where we
    reject \(H_0\) with probability \(\gamma\).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  More generally, \(\phi(x)\) is just a function of \(x\) with values in
  \([0,1]\), which represents the probability that we will reject
  \(H_0\).
\end{itemize}

\begin{example}[Randomized Test for
Binomial]\protect\hypertarget{exm-randomized-test}{}\label{exm-randomized-test}

Let \(X \sim \text{Bin}(n=10, \theta)\). Consider testing
\(H_0: \theta = 1/2\) vs \(H_1: \theta > 1/2\) with target size
\(\alpha = 0.05\).

Suppose we choose a critical region \(X \ge k\).

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  If \(k=9\), \(P(X \ge 9 | \theta=0.5) \approx 0.0107\).
\item
  If \(k=8\), \(P(X \ge 8 | \theta=0.5) \approx 0.0547\).
\end{itemize}

Since we cannot achieve exactly 0.05 with a non-randomized test (the
survival function jumps over 0.05), we must use a randomized test
function.

The randomized test is defined as:

\[
\phi(x) = \begin{cases} 
1 & \text{if } x \in C_1 \text{ (i.e., } x \ge 9) \\
\gamma & \text{if } x \in C_* \text{ (i.e., } x = 8) \\
0 & \text{otherwise}
\end{cases}
\]

From the figure, we see that \(\alpha = 0.05\) lies between
\(P(X \ge 9)\) and \(P(X \ge 8)\). We always reject the ``tail'' where
probabilities are strictly less than \(\alpha\) (here \(x \ge 9\)). At
the boundary \(x=8\), we cannot reject with probability 1 (which would
give total size 0.0547), nor with probability 0 (which would give total
size 0.0107).

We choose \(\gamma\) to bridge this gap:

\[
\begin{aligned}
\alpha &= P(X \ge 9) + \gamma \cdot P(X = 8) \\
0.05 &= 0.01074 + \gamma \cdot (P(X \ge 8) - P(X \ge 9)) \\
0.05 &= 0.01074 + \gamma \cdot (0.05469 - 0.01074)
\end{aligned}
\]

Solving for \(\gamma\):

\[
\gamma = \frac{0.05 - 0.01074}{0.04395} \approx \frac{39}{44} \approx 0.89
\]

\end{example}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{hypothesis_files/figure-pdf/fig-binomial-survival-zoom-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-binomial-survival-zoom}Survival Function P(X
\textgreater= k) with randomization detail}

\end{figure}%

\subsection{Size}\label{size}

\begin{definition}[Size of a
Test]\protect\hypertarget{def-test-size}{}\label{def-test-size}

The \textbf{size} of a test \(\phi(x)\) is the maximum probability of
rejecting the null hypothesis when it is true:

\[
\text{Size}(\phi) = \sup_{\theta \in \Theta_0} W_\phi(\theta) = \sup_{\theta \in \Theta_0} E_\theta[\phi(X)]
\]

\end{definition}

\subsection{Power}\label{power}

We distinguish between the power function varying over parameters and
the power metric of a specific test.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Power Function (\(W_\phi(\theta)\))} The probability of
  rejecting \(H_0\) as a function of the parameter \(\theta\):
\end{enumerate}

\[
W_\phi(\theta) = E_\theta[\phi(X)]
\]

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{1}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Power of the Test (\(\text{Power}(\phi)\))} In the context of
  a specific alternative hypothesis (e.g., \(H_1: \theta = \theta_1\)),
  we define the power as a scalar functional of \(\phi\):
\end{enumerate}

\[
\text{Power}(\phi) = E_{\theta_1}[\phi(X)]
\]

Ideally, we want:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(W_\phi(\theta) \le \text{Size}(\phi)\) for all
  \(\theta \in \Theta_0\) (Control Type I error).
\item
  \(\text{Power}(\phi)\) to be as large as possible (Maximize
  sensitivity to \(H_1\)).
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{hypothesis_files/figure-pdf/fig-test-function-size-power-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-test-function-size-power}Illustration of a Test
Function \(\phi(x)\) (Pink) relative to Size (\(H_0\), Blue) and Power
(\(H_1\), Red).}

\end{figure}%

\section{The Neyman-Pearson Lemma}\label{the-neyman-pearson-lemma}

Consider testing a simple null against a simple alternative:
\(H_0: \theta = \theta_0\) vs \(H_1: \theta = \theta_1\).

We define the \textbf{Likelihood Ratio} \(\Lambda(x)\) as:

\[
\Lambda(x) = \frac{f_1(x)}{f_0(x)} = \frac{f(x; \theta_1)}{f(x; \theta_0)}
\]

\begin{definition}[Likelihood Ratio Test
(LRT)]\protect\hypertarget{def-lrt}{}\label{def-lrt}

A test \(\phi(x)\) is a Likelihood Ratio Test if it has the form:

\[
\phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) = \begin{cases} 
1 & \text{if } \Lambda(x) > k \\
\gamma(x) & \text{if } \Lambda(x) = k \\
0 & \text{if } \Lambda(x) < k
\end{cases}
\]

where \(k \ge 0\) is a constant and \(0 \le \gamma(x) \le 1\).

\end{definition}

\subsection{Neyman-Pearson Lemma}\label{neyman-pearson-lemma}

\begin{theorem}[Neyman-Pearson
Lemma]\protect\hypertarget{thm-neyman-pearson}{}\label{thm-neyman-pearson}

~

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\alph{enumi})}
\item
  \textbf{Optimality:} For any \(k\) and \(\gamma(x)\), the LRT
  \(\phi_0(x)\) defined above has maximum power among all tests whose
  size is less than or equal to the size of \(\phi_0(x)\).
\item
  \textbf{Existence:} Given \(\alpha \in (0, 1)\), there exist constants
  \(k\) and \(\gamma_0\) such that the LRT defined by this \(k\) and
  \(\gamma(x) = \gamma_0\) has size exactly \(\alpha\).
\item
  \textbf{Uniqueness:} If a test \(\phi\) has size \(\alpha\) and is of
  maximum power among all tests of size \(\alpha\), then \(\phi\) is
  necessarily an LRT, except possibly on a set of measure zero under
  \(H_0\) and \(H_1\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{theorem}

\subsection{A Derivation with The Lagrange Multiplier
Approach}\label{a-derivation-with-the-lagrange-multiplier-approach}

To make the optimality of the Likelihood Ratio Test (LRT) intuitive, we
can frame the search for the best test function \(\phi(x)\) as a
constrained optimization problem.

We want to maximize the power of the test:

\[
\text{Power}(\phi) = \int \phi(x) f_1(x) dx
\]

subject to the constraint on the size of the test \(\alpha\):

\[
\text{Size}(\phi) = \int \phi(x) f_0(x) dx = \alpha
\]

Using the method of Lagrange multipliers, we define the objective
function \(L\) with a multiplier \(k\):

\[
L(\phi, k) = \int \phi(x) f_1(x) dx - k \left( \int \phi(x) f_0(x) dx - \alpha \right)
\]

Rearranging the terms inside the integral, we get:

\[
L(\phi, k) = \int \phi(x) [f_1(x) - k f_0(x)] dx + k\alpha
\]

To maximize \(L\) with respect to \(\phi(x)\), we look at the integrand.
Since \(0 \le \phi(x) \le 1\), we should choose \(\phi(x)\) to be as
large as possible whenever its coefficient is positive, and as small as
possible whenever its coefficient is negative:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  If \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) > 0\), set \(\phi(x) = 1\).
\item
  If \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) < 0\), set \(\phi(x) = 0\).
\item
  If \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) = 0\), the value of \(\phi(x)\) does not affect
  the integral (this is where \(\gamma\) comes in).
\end{itemize}

This decision rule is equivalent to:

\[
\phi(x) = 
\begin{cases} 
1 & \text{if } \frac{f_1(x)}{f_0(x)} > k \\
0 & \text{if } \frac{f_1(x)}{f_0(x)} < k
\end{cases}
\]

This is precisely the form of the Likelihood Ratio Test. The ``shadow
price'' or Lagrange multiplier \(k\) represents the critical threshold
that balances the gain in power against the cost of increasing the Type
I error.

\subsection{Proof of NP Lemma}\label{proof-of-np-lemma}

\begin{proof}
\textbf{Proof of (a) Optimality:} Let \(\phi_{\text{LRT}}\) be the LRT
with size \(\alpha\), and \(\phi\) be any other test with size
\(\le \alpha\). Define the function \(U(x)\) as the difference in test
functions weighted by the linear combination of densities:

\[
U(x) = (\phi_{}(x) - \phi(x))(f_1(x) - k f_0(x))
\]

We analyze the sign of \(U(x)\) by looking at the sign of its two
factors in three cases:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  If \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) > 0\) (implies \(\Lambda(x) > k\)). Since
  \(\phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) = 1\) and \(\phi(x) \le 1\), we have:

  \[
    \begin{aligned}
    \phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) - \phi(x) &\ge 0 \\
    U(x) = (\phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) - \phi(x))(f_1(x) - k f_0(x)) &\ge 0
    \end{aligned}
    \]
\item
  If \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) < 0\) (implies \(\Lambda(x) < k\)). Since
  \(\phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) = 0\) and \(\phi(x) \ge 0\), we have:

  \[
    \begin{aligned}
    \phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) - \phi(x) &\le 0 \\
    U(x) = (\phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) - \phi(x))(f_1(x) - k f_0(x)) &\ge 0
    \end{aligned}
    \]
\item
  If \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) = 0\). The product is zero regardless of the
  test functions.

  \[
    U(x) = 0
    \]
\end{itemize}

Combining these cases, we conclude that the product is non-negative for
all \(x\):

\[
U(x) = (\phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) - \phi(x))(f_1(x) - k f_0(x)) \ge 0
\]

Therefore, integrating \(U(x)\) over the entire domain:

\[
\int U(x) dx = \int (\phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) - \phi(x))(f_1(x) - k f_0(x)) dx \ge 0
\]

Expanding the integral:

\[
\int \phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) f_1(x) \, dx - \int \phi(x) f_1(x) \, dx - k \left( \int \phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) f_0(x) \, dx - \int \phi(x) f_0(x) \, dx \right) \ge 0
\] Converting to expectations:

\[
E_{\theta_1}[\phi_{\text{LRT}}] - E_{\theta_1}[\phi] \ge k (E_{\theta_0}[\phi_{\text{LRT}}] - E_{\theta_0}[\phi])  
\]

Since
\(E_{\theta_0}[\phi_{\text{LRT}}] =\text{Size}(\phi_{\text{LRT}})= \alpha\)
and we require that
\(E_{\theta_0}[\phi] = \text{Size}(\phi)\le \alpha\),\\
\[
E_{\theta_0}[\phi_{\text{LRT}}] - E_{\theta_0}[\phi] \ge 0
\]

Thereore, given that \(k \ge 0\):

\[
\text{Power}(\phi_{\text{LRT}}) \ge \text{Power}(\phi)
\]

\textbf{Proof of (b) Existence:}

Let \(G(k) = P_{\theta_0}(\Lambda(X) \le k)\). \(G(k)\) is the
cumulative distribution function of the random variable \(\Lambda(X)\),
so it is non-decreasing. We seek \(k_0\) such that
\(1 - G(k_0) \approx \alpha\). Because of discrete jumps, we might not
hit \(\alpha\) exactly. We choose \(k_0\) such that:

\[
P_{\theta_0}(\Lambda(X) > k_0) \le \alpha \le P_{\theta_0}(\Lambda(X) \ge k_0)
\]

Set
\(\gamma_0 = \frac{\alpha - P_{\theta_0}(\Lambda(X) > k_0)}{P_{\theta_0}(\Lambda(X) = k_0)}\).

\textbf{Proof of (c) Uniqueness}

Let \(\phi_{\text{LRT}}\) be the LRT of size \(\alpha\). Suppose there
exists another test \(\phi\) that is also Most Powerful (MP) with size
\(\le \alpha\). We wish to show that \(\phi(x) = \phi_{\text{LRT}}(x)\)
for almost all \(x\) where \(f_1(x) \ne k f_0(x)\).

As established in the optimality proof, the function: \[
U(x) = (\phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) - \phi(x))(f_1(x) - k f_0(x))
\] is non-negative for all \(x\). Since both tests are MP, they have the
same power: \(E_{\theta_1}[\phi_{\text{LRT}}] = E_{\theta_1}[\phi]\).

From the integral of \(U(x)\), we have: \[
0 \le \int U(x) dx = (E_{\theta_1}[\phi_{\text{LRT}}] - E_{\theta_1}[\phi]) - k(E_{\theta_0}[\phi_{\text{LRT}}] - E_{\theta_0}[\phi])
\]

Substituting the equality of power: \[
0 \le -k(\alpha - E_{\theta_0}[\phi])
\]

Since \(k > 0\) and \(E_{\theta_0}[\phi] \le \alpha\), the term
\(-k(\alpha - E_{\theta_0}[\phi])\) is \(\le 0\). The only way for the
integral of a non-negative function \(U(x)\) to be \(\le 0\) is if the
integral is exactly zero: \[
\int (\phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) - \phi(x))(f_1(x) - k f_0(x)) \, dx = 0
\]

For the integral of a non-negative function to be zero, the integrand
must be zero almost everywhere: \[
(\phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) - \phi(x))(f_1(x) - k f_0(x)) = 0 \quad \text{a.e.}
\]

This implies that for any \(x\) where \(f_1(x) - k f_0(x) \ne 0\), we
must have: \[
\phi_{\text{LRT}}(x) - \phi(x) = 0 \implies \phi(x) = \phi_{\text{LRT}}(x)
\]

Thus, the test is unique except possibly on the boundary set
\(\{x : f_1(x) = k f_0(x)\}\). If \(P_{\theta_0}(\Lambda(X) = k) = 0\)
(as in continuous distributions like the Normal), the MP test is unique
almost everywhere.
\end{proof}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{hypothesis_files/figure-pdf/fig-NPlemma-proof-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-NPlemma-proof}Visualizing the NP Lemma. The thick,
transparent pink line is \(\phi_{ ext{LRT}}\). The thin solid black line
is \(\phi\). Overlap is visible as a black line inside pink.}

\end{figure}%

\section{Uniformly Most Powerful (UMP)
Tests}\label{uniformly-most-powerful-ump-tests}

When the alternative hypothesis is composite
(\(H_1: \theta \in \Theta_1\)), we seek a test that is ``best'' for
\emph{all} \(\theta \in \Theta_1\).

\begin{definition}[Uniformly Most Powerful
Test]\protect\hypertarget{def-ump}{}\label{def-ump}

A test \(\phi_0(x)\) of size \(\alpha\) is \textbf{Uniformly Most
Powerful (UMP)} if:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \(E_{\theta}[\phi_0(X)] \le \alpha\) for all \(\theta \in \Theta_0\).
\item
  For any other test \(\phi(x)\) satisfying (1),
  \(E_{\theta}[\phi_0(X)] \ge E_{\theta}[\phi(X)]\) for all
  \(\theta \in \Theta_1\).
\end{enumerate}

\end{definition}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{hypothesis_files/figure-pdf/fig-ump-power-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-ump-power}Diagram of UMP Tests}

\end{figure}%

\section{Monotone Likelihood Ratio
(MLR)}\label{monotone-likelihood-ratio-mlr}

\begin{definition}[Monotone Likelihood
Ratio]\protect\hypertarget{def-mlr}{}\label{def-mlr}

A family of densities \(\{f(x; \theta)\}\) has a \textbf{Monotone
Likelihood Ratio (MLR)} with respect to a statistic \(T(x)\) if for any
\(\theta_1 > \theta_0\), the ratio:

\[
\frac{f(x; \theta_1)}{f(x; \theta_0)}
\]

is a non-decreasing function of \(T(x)\).

\end{definition}

Common examples include the one-parameter Exponential Family:
\(f(x; \theta) = h(x) c(\theta) \exp\{w(\theta) T(x)\}\). If
\(w(\theta)\) is increasing, the family has MLR w.r.t \(T(x)\).

\begin{example}[]\protect\hypertarget{exm-mlr-gamma}{}\label{exm-mlr-gamma}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \overset{iid}{\sim} \text{Exp}(\theta)\) with pdf
\(f(x) = \frac{1}{\theta} e^{-x/\theta}\). Test
\[H_0: \theta = \theta_0 \text{ vs } H_1: \theta > \theta_0.\]

The Likelihood Ratio for \(\theta_1 > \theta_0\) is:

\[
\frac{L(\theta_1)}{L(\theta_0)} = \frac{\theta_1^{-n} e^{-\sum x_i / \theta_1}}{\theta_0^{-n} e^{-\sum x_i / \theta_0}} = \left(\frac{\theta_0}{\theta_1}\right)^n \exp \left\{ \left( \frac{1}{\theta_0} - \frac{1}{\theta_1} \right) \sum x_i \right\}
\]

Since \(\theta_1 > \theta_0\), the term
\((\frac{1}{\theta_0} - \frac{1}{\theta_1})\) is positive. Thus,
\(\Lambda(x)\) is an increasing function of the sum
\(T (x) = \sum x_i\).

Rejecting for large \(\Lambda(x)\) is equivalent to rejecting for
\(T(x)=\sum x_i > C\).

Under \(H_0\), \(X_i \sim \text{Exp}(\theta_0)\), which is equivalent to
\(\text{Gamma}(1, \theta_0)\). By the reproductive property of the Gamma
distribution:

\[T = \sum_{i=1}^n X_i \sim \text{Gamma}(n, \theta_0)\]

Alternatively, using the relationship with the Chi-square distribution:

\[\frac{2T}{\theta_0} \sim \chi^2_{2n}\]

To find \(C\) for a significance level \(\alpha\), we set
\(P(T > C | \theta_0) = \alpha\). Using the \(\chi^2\) transformation:

\[P\left(\frac{2T}{\theta_0} > \frac{2C}{\theta_0}\right) = \alpha \implies \frac{2C}{\theta_0} = \chi^2_{2n, \alpha}\]

Thus, the critical value is:

\[C = \frac{\theta_0}{2} \chi^2_{2n, \alpha}\]

where \(\chi^2_{2n, \alpha}\) is the upper-\(\alpha\) quantile of a
Chi-square distribution with \(2n\) degrees of freedom.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, titlerule=0mm, coltitle=black, opacitybacktitle=0.6, bottomtitle=1mm, colframe=quarto-callout-important-color-frame, bottomrule=.15mm, arc=.35mm, opacityback=0, colback=white, leftrule=.75mm, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-important-color}{\faExclamation}\hspace{0.5em}{Important}, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm, toptitle=1mm, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-important-color!10!white, breakable, left=2mm]

We note that the value \(C\) does not depend on \(\theta_1\).

\end{tcolorbox}

\end{example}

\subsection{Karlin-Rubin Theorem}\label{karlin-rubin-theorem}

\begin{theorem}[Chebyshev's Association
Inequality]\protect\hypertarget{thm-chebyshev-covariance}{}\label{thm-chebyshev-covariance}

~

\begin{theorem}

Let \(X\) be a random variable, and let \(f(x)\) and \(g(x)\) be two
functions that are both non-decreasing (or both non-increasing). Then:
\[
E[f(X)g(X)] \ge E[f(X)] \cdot E[g(X)]
\] Equivalently, the covariance is non-negative:
\(\text{Cov}(f(X), g(X)) \ge 0\).

\end{theorem}

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Let \(Y\) be an independent copy of \(X\) (i.e., \(X\) and \(Y\) are
i.i.d.). Consider the quantity: \[
\Delta = (f(X) - f(Y))(g(X) - g(Y))
\]

Since \(f\) and \(g\) are both non-decreasing (or both non-increasing),
the terms \((f(X) - f(Y))\) and \((g(X) - g(Y))\) always share the same
sign. Thus, their product is always non-negative: \[
\Delta \ge 0
\]

Taking the expectation: \[
E[(f(X) - f(Y))(g(X) - g(Y))] \ge 0
\]

Expanding the product and using linearity of expectation: \[
E[f(X)g(X)] - E[f(X)g(Y)] - E[f(Y)g(X)] + E[f(Y)g(Y)] \ge 0
\]

Since \(X\) and \(Y\) are i.i.d.:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \(E[f(Y)g(Y)] = E[f(X)g(X)]\)
\item
  \(E[f(X)g(Y)] = E[f(X)]E[g(Y)] = E[f(X)]E[g(X)]\) (Independence)
\item
  \(E[f(Y)g(X)] = E[f(Y)]E[g(X)] = E[f(X)]E[g(X)]\) (Independence)
\end{enumerate}

Substituting these back yields: \[
2E[f(X)g(X)] - 2E[f(X)]E[g(X)] \ge 0
\]

Dividing by 2 proves the inequality: \[
E[f(X)g(X)] \ge E[f(X)]E[g(X)]
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Karlin-Rubin
Theorem]\protect\hypertarget{thm-karlin-rubin}{}\label{thm-karlin-rubin}

Suppose \(X\) has a distribution from a family with MLR with respect to
\(T(X)\), and the distribution of \(T(X)\) is continuous. Consider
testing \(H_0: \theta \le \theta_0\) vs \(H_1: \theta > \theta_0\).

The test:

\[
\phi(x) = \begin{cases} 
1 & \text{if } T(x) > t_0 \\
0 & \text{if } T(x) \le t_0
\end{cases}
\]

where \(t_0\) is determined by \(P_{\theta_0}(T(X) > t_0) = \alpha\), is
the UMP size \(\alpha\) test.

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\textbf{The Test:} Define the test \(\phi_0(x)\) as:

\[
\phi_0(x) = \begin{cases} 
1 & \text{if } T(x) > t_0 \\
0 & \text{if } T(x) \le t_0
\end{cases}
\]

where \(t_0\) is determined such that the power at the boundary is
\(\alpha\), i.e., \(W_{\phi_0}(\theta_0) = \alpha\).

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Monotonicity of the Power Function}

  We first establish that \(W_{\phi_0}(\theta)\) is non-decreasing. Let
  \(\theta_1 > \theta_0\). Define the test indicator function
  \(h(t) = \mathbb{I}(t > t_0)\) and the likelihood ratio
  \(\Lambda(t) = \frac{f_{\theta_1}(t)}{f_{\theta_0}(t)}\).

  Because of the Monotone Likelihood Ratio (MLR) property,
  \(\Lambda(t)\) is a non-decreasing function of \(t\). The indicator
  function \(h(t)\) is clearly non-decreasing.

  The power at \(\theta_1\) can be written as an integral involving the
  density under \(\theta_0\): \[
  W_{\phi_0}(\theta_1) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} h(t) f_{\theta_1}(t) \, dt = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} h(t) \frac{f_{\theta_1}(t)}{f_{\theta_0}(t)} f_{\theta_0}(t) \, dt = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} h(t) \Lambda(t) f_{\theta_0}(t) \, dt
  \]

  This integral is the expectation \(E_{\theta_0}[h(T) \Lambda(T)]\). By
  Chebyshev's Association Inequality (Covariance Inequality), since both
  \(h(t)\) and \(\Lambda(t)\) are non-decreasing, the expectation of
  their product is at least the product of their expectations: \[
  \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} h(t) \Lambda(t) f_{\theta_0}(t) \, dt \ge \left( \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} h(t) f_{\theta_0}(t) \, dt \right) \left( \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \Lambda(t) f_{\theta_0}(t) \, dt \right)
  \]

  We evaluate the two integrals on the right-hand side:

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\arabic{enumii}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    The first term is the power at \(\theta_0\):
    \(\int h(t) f_{\theta_0}(t) \, dt = W_{\phi_0}(\theta_0)\).
  \item
    The second term integrates the likelihood ratio:
    \(\int \frac{f_{\theta_1}(t)}{f_{\theta_0}(t)} f_{\theta_0}(t) \, dt = \int f_{\theta_1}(t) \, dt = 1\).
  \end{enumerate}

  Substituting these back, we get: \[
  W_{\phi_0}(\theta_1) \ge W_{\phi_0}(\theta_0) \cdot 1
  \] Thus, \(W_{\phi_0}(\theta)\) is non-decreasing.
\item
  \textbf{Size Control}

  For the composite null \(H_0: \theta \le \theta_0\), we require the
  size to be at most \(\alpha\). Since \(W_{\phi_0}(\theta)\) is
  non-decreasing (from Step 1) and we explicitly set
  \(W_{\phi_0}(\theta_0) = \alpha\):

  \[
  W_{\phi_0}(\theta) \le W_{\phi_0}(\theta_0) = \alpha \quad \text{for all } \theta \le \theta_0
  \]

  This confirms \(\phi_0\) is a valid level-\(\alpha\) test.
\item
  \textbf{Uniformly Most Powerful (UMP) via Neyman-Pearson Lemma}

  Let \(\phi'(x)\) be any other valid test of size \(\alpha\) for
  \(H_0\). This implies \(W_{\phi'}(\theta_0) \le \alpha\).

  Consider any specific alternative \(\theta_1 > \theta_0\). Because the
  family has MLR, the likelihood ratio \(\Lambda(x)\) is increasing in
  \(T(x)\). Therefore, the test \(\phi_0\) (which rejects for large
  \(T\)) is identified by the Neyman-Pearson Lemma as the Most Powerful
  (MP) test for the simple hypothesis \(\theta_0\) vs \(\theta_1\).

  Comparing the power of \(\phi_0\) and \(\phi'\) at this specific
  \(\theta_1\): \[
  W_{\phi_0}(\theta_1) \ge W_{\phi'}(\theta_1)
  \] Since \(\theta_1\) was arbitrary, this inequality holds for all
  \(\theta > \theta_0\). Thus, \(\phi_0\) is the UMP test.
\end{enumerate}

\end{proof}

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{hypothesis_files/figure-pdf/fig-monotone-power-MLR-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-monotone-power-MLR}Visualizing Monotonicity of Power
Function \(W(\theta)\) for MLR Distributions}

\end{figure}%

\begin{example}[UMP Test for
Exponential/Gamma]\protect\hypertarget{exm-ump-gamma}{}\label{exm-ump-gamma}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \overset{iid}{\sim} \text{Exp}(\theta)\) with pdf
\(f(x) = \frac{1}{\theta} e^{-x/\theta}\). Test
\(H_0: \theta = \theta_0\) vs \(H_1: \theta > \theta_0\). The sum
\(T = \sum X_i\) is a sufficient statistic, and
\(T \sim \text{Gamma}(n, \theta)\).

The Likelihood Ratio for \(\theta_1 > \theta_0\) is:

\[
\frac{L(\theta_1)}{L(\theta_0)} = \frac{\theta_1^{-n} e^{-\sum x_i / \theta_1}}{\theta_0^{-n} e^{-\sum x_i / \theta_0}} = \left(\frac{\theta_0}{\theta_1}\right)^n \exp \left\{ \left( \frac{1}{\theta_0} - \frac{1}{\theta_1} \right) \sum x_i \right\}
\]

Since \(\theta_1 > \theta_0\), the term
\((\frac{1}{\theta_0} - \frac{1}{\theta_1})\) is positive. Thus,
\(\Lambda(x)\) is an increasing function of \(\sum x_i\).

Rejecting for large \(\Lambda(x)\) is equivalent to rejecting for
\(\sum x_i > C\).

\(T \sim \text{Gamma}(n, \theta)\)

This test form does not depend on the specific \(\theta_1\), so it is
UMP for all \(\theta > \theta_0\).

\end{example}

\subsection{Non-Existence of UMP for Two-Sided
Hypotheses}\label{non-existence-of-ump-for-two-sided-hypotheses}

For testing a point null hypothesis \(H_0: \theta = \theta_0\) against a
two-sided alternative \(H_1: \theta \neq \theta_0\) in a family with a
monotone likelihood ratio (e.g., Normal, Exponential), a Uniformly Most
Powerful (UMP) test generally \textbf{does not exist}. The non-existence
proof relies on the uniqueness of the Most Powerful (MP) test derived
from the Neyman-Pearson Lemma:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Conflict of Optimal Regions:} Consider a specific alternative
  \(\theta_1 > \theta_0\). By the Neyman-Pearson Lemma, the MP test
  \(\phi_1\) rejects \(H_0\) for large values of the sufficient
  statistic \(T(\mathbf{X}) > k_1\). Conversely, consider an alternative
  \(\theta_2 < \theta_0\). The MP test \(\phi_2\) rejects \(H_0\) for
  small values of the statistic \(T(\mathbf{X}) < k_2\).
\item
  \textbf{Failure of Uniformity:} A UMP test \(\phi^*\) would need to be
  the MP test for \emph{every} \(\theta \in H_1\).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    For \(\phi^*\) to be most powerful against \(\theta_1\), it must be
    equivalent to \(\phi_1\) (rejecting in the right tail).
  \item
    For \(\phi^*\) to be most powerful against \(\theta_2\), it must be
    equivalent to \(\phi_2\) (rejecting in the left tail).
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Biased Power Function:} The MP test \(\phi_1\) (Right-Sided)
  has a power function that drops below the size \(\alpha\) for values
  \(\theta < \theta_0\). Therefore, it cannot be the most powerful test
  for \(\theta_2\), as there exists a valid test (e.g., \(\phi_2\)) with
  power strictly greater than \(\alpha\) at \(\theta_2\).
\end{enumerate}

Since no single critical region can simultaneously maximize power for
both \(\theta > \theta_0\) and \(\theta < \theta_0\), no UMP test
exists. We typically restrict our search to \textbf{Unbiased} tests
(UMPU) to resolve this.

\begin{figure}

\centering{

\pandocbounded{\includegraphics[keepaspectratio]{hypothesis_files/figure-pdf/fig-two-powers-normal-1.pdf}}

}

\caption{\label{fig-two-powers-normal}Illustration of Non-existence of
UMP Tests for Two-sided Tests}

\end{figure}%

\section{Likelihood Ratio Tests}\label{likelihood-ratio-tests}

The Likelihood Ratio Test (LRT) provides a unified approach for
constructing hypothesis tests for parametric models. Let
\(\mathbf{Y} = (Y_1, \dots, Y_n)\) be a random sample with joint
probability density function \(L(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{Y})\),
where \(\boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta\). We consider the general
hypothesis testing problem:
\[H_0: \boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta_0 \quad \text{vs} \quad H_1: \boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta \setminus \Theta_0\]

The \textbf{Likelihood Ratio Statistic} is defined as the ratio of the
maximum likelihood achievable under the null hypothesis to the maximum
likelihood achievable under the full parameter space:

\[\Lambda(\mathbf{Y}) = \frac{\sup_{\boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta_0} L(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{Y})}{\sup_{\boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta} L(\boldsymbol{\theta}; \mathbf{Y})} = \frac{L(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)})}{L(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)})}\]

where \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\) is the restricted MLE and
\(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)}\) is the unrestricted (global) MLE. By
definition, the likelihood ratio is bounded by
\(0 \le \Lambda(\mathbf{Y}) \le 1\). The Likelihood Ratio Test (LRT) is
defined by the decision function:

\[
\phi(\mathbf{Y}) = \begin{cases} 
1 & \text{if } \Lambda(\mathbf{Y}) \le c \quad (\text{Reject } H_0) \\
0 & \text{if } \Lambda(\mathbf{Y}) > c \quad (\text{Fail to reject } H_0)
\end{cases}
\]

where \(c \in (0, 1)\) is a critical value determined by the
significance level \(\alpha\). This rejection region
\(\{\mathbf{Y} : \Lambda(\mathbf{Y}) \le c\}\) corresponds to cases
where the restricted likelihood (under \(H_0\)) is significantly smaller
than the unrestricted likelihood.

\subsection{Geometry of Likelihood}\label{geometry-of-likelihood}

Before deriving the asymptotic distribution of the LRT, we establish the
geometric framework. We define the inner product on the parameter space
induced by the Fisher Information \(\mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\)
as
\(\langle \mathbf{u}, \mathbf{v} \rangle_{\mathcal{I}} = \mathbf{u}^T \mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) \mathbf{v}\).

\begin{definition}[Definition: Orthogonal Projection under Fisher
Information]\protect\hypertarget{def-orthogonality}{}\label{def-orthogonality}

Let \(\Theta_0\) be a linear subspace of the parameter space. The
\textbf{orthogonal projection} of a point
\(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)}\) onto \(\Theta_0\) with respect to
the inner product \(\langle \cdot, \cdot \rangle_{\mathcal{I}}\) is the
unique vector \(\tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)} \in \Theta_0\) such
that the residual is orthogonal to the subspace:
\[ \langle \hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)} - \tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}, \mathbf{v} \rangle_{\mathcal{I}} = 0 \quad \text{for all } \mathbf{v} \in \Theta_0 \]

\textbf{Consequence (Pythagorean Identity):} As a direct consequence of
this definition, for any \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0 \in \Theta_0\), the
vector \((\tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\)
lies in \(\Theta_0\) and is therefore orthogonal to the residual
\((\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)} - \tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)})\).
This implies the Pythagorean relationship holds exactly:
\[\|\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0\|_{\mathcal{I}}^2 = \|\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)} - \tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\|_{\mathcal{I}}^2 + \|\tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0\|_{\mathcal{I}}^2\]

\end{definition}

\subsection{Wilks' Theorem}\label{wilks-theorem-1}

The asymptotic distribution of the Likelihood Ratio Test statistic is
given by the celebrated result from Wilks (1938). This theorem allows us
to construct hypothesis tests without deriving the exact distribution of
the likelihood ratio \(\Lambda\) for every specific model.

\begin{theorem}[Wilks'
Theorem]\protect\hypertarget{thm-wilks}{}\label{thm-wilks}

Let \(\mathbf{Y} = (Y_1, \dots, Y_n)\) be an i.i.d. sample from a
regular family of distributions with parameters
\(\boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta \subseteq \mathbb{R}^{p_1}\). Let
\(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\) denote the true parameter value. Consider the
test:
\[H_0: \boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta_0 \quad \text{vs} \quad H_1: \boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta_1\]
where \(\Theta_0 \subset \Theta_1\) are smooth parameter spaces with
dimensions \(p_0 < p_1\). Let \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)}\) be the
unrestricted MLE under \(H_1\), and \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\)
be the restricted MLE under \(H_0\).

The likelihood ratio statistic is
\(D = 2[\ell(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)}) - \ell(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)})]\).
Under \(H_0\), as \(n \to \infty\): \[D \xrightarrow{d} \chi^2_{k}\]
where \(k = p_1 - p_0\) is the difference in dimensionality between the
models.

\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\textbf{Geometric Proof via Quadratic Approximation}

We rely on the geometric interpretation of the log-likelihood function
as a squared distance metric induced by the Fisher Information matrix
\(\mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\).

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Quadratic Approximation of the Log-Likelihood}

  We expand the log-likelihood \(\ell(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) around the
  unrestricted MLE \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)}\). Since
  \(\nabla \ell(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)}) = \mathbf{0}\), the
  linear term vanishes. Asymptotically, the Hessian converges to
  \(-n\mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0)\):
  \[\ell(\boldsymbol{\theta}) \approx \ell(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)}) - \frac{n}{2} (\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)} - \boldsymbol{\theta})^T \mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0) (\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)} - \boldsymbol{\theta})\]
  \[\ell(\boldsymbol{\theta}) \approx \ell(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)}) - \frac{n}{2} \|\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)} - \boldsymbol{\theta}\|_{\mathcal{I}}^2\]

  This approximation replaces the likelihood surface with a parabola
  \(Q(\boldsymbol{\theta})\).
\item
  \textbf{Approximation of the Restricted Estimator (Projection)}

  The true restricted MLE \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\) maximizes
  the \emph{exact} log-likelihood \(\ell(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) over
  \(\Theta_0\). However, for the quadratic approximation, we define the
  \textbf{Approximate Restricted MLE}
  \(\tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\) as the maximizer of the
  \emph{parabolic} approximation \(Q(\boldsymbol{\theta})\) over
  \(\Theta_0\). This is equivalent to minimizing the distance to the
  unrestricted MLE:
  \[\tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)} = \arg\min_{\boldsymbol{\theta} \in \Theta_0} \|\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)} - \boldsymbol{\theta}\|_{\mathcal{I}}^2\]

  Geometrically, \(\tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\) is the
  \textbf{orthogonal projection} (as defined in
  Definition~\ref{def-orthogonality}) of
  \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)}\) onto the subspace \(\Theta_0\)
  with respect to the metric \(\mathcal{I}\).

  \textbf{Crucial Justification:} Why can we replace
  \(\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\) with
  \(\tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\)? According to \textbf{Le Cam's
  Local Asymptotic Normality (LAN)} theory (Le Cam, 1986), the
  likelihood surface becomes locally quadratic and the parameter space
  locally linear. Consequently, the difference between the true
  restricted maximum and the quadratic projection vanishes at a rate
  faster than the estimation error:
  \[\|\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)} - \tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\| = o_p(n^{-1/2})\]
  Thus, for the purpose of the asymptotic distribution, we use
  \(\tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\).
\item
  \textbf{Orthogonal Decomposition}

  Substituting the projection \(\tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\) into
  the test statistic approximation:
  \[D \approx n \|\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)} - \tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\|_{\mathcal{I}}^2\]

  Using the \textbf{Pythagorean Identity} from
  Definition~\ref{def-orthogonality} with \(\boldsymbol{\theta}_0\) as
  the true parameter (which lies in \(\Theta_0\) under the null
  hypothesis):
  \[n \|\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)} - \tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)}\|_{\mathcal{I}}^2 = n \|\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0\|_{\mathcal{I}}^2 - n \|\tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0\|_{\mathcal{I}}^2\]
\item
  \textbf{Distributional Conclusion}

  Multiplying by \(n\), we identify the terms with Chi-square variables:
  \[\underbrace{D}_{\text{Statistic}} \approx \underbrace{n\|\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(1)} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0\|_{\mathcal{I}}^2}_{\sim \chi^2_{p_1}} - \underbrace{n\|\tilde{\boldsymbol{\theta}}^{(0)} - \boldsymbol{\theta}_0\|_{\mathcal{I}}^2}_{\sim \chi^2_{p_0}}\]

  By Cochran's Theorem, the squared length of the projection of a
  standard normal vector onto the orthogonal complement of a subspace of
  dimension \(p_0\) is distributed as a Chi-square with degrees of
  freedom equal to the codimension:
  \[D \xrightarrow{d} \chi^2_{p_1 - p_0}\]
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{References:} Le Cam, L. (1986). \emph{Asymptotic Methods in
Statistical Decision Theory}. Springer-Verlag.
\end{proof}

\begin{example}[]\protect\hypertarget{exm-lrt-normal}{}\label{exm-lrt-normal}

\textbf{LRT for Normal Mean with Unknown Variance}

Let \(X_1, \dots, X_n \overset{i.i.d.}{\sim} N(\mu, \sigma^2)\) where
both \(\mu\) and \(\sigma^2\) are unknown. The parameter vector is
\(\theta = (\mu, \sigma^2)\). We wish to test:
\[H_0: \mu = \mu_0 \quad \text{vs} \quad H_1: \mu \neq \mu_0\]

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Find the Maximum Likelihood Estimators:} The log-likelihood
  function is
  \(\ell(\mu, \sigma^2) = -\frac{n}{2}\ln(2\pi) - \frac{n}{2}\ln(\sigma^2) - \frac{1}{2\sigma^2}\sum_{i=1}^n (x_i - \mu)^2\).

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    \textbf{Unrestricted MLEs (\(\hat{\theta}\)):}
    \(\hat{\mu} = \bar{X}, \quad \hat{\sigma}^2 = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n (X_i - \bar{X})^2\).
    The maximized log-likelihood is:
    \[\ell(\hat{\mu}, \hat{\sigma}^2) = -\frac{n}{2}\ln(2\pi) - \frac{n}{2}\ln(\hat{\sigma}^2) - \frac{n}{2}\]
  \item
    \textbf{Restricted MLEs (\(\hat{\theta}_0\)):} Under \(H_0\),
    \(\hat{\mu}_0 = \mu_0, \quad \hat{\sigma}_0^2 = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n (X_i - \mu_0)^2\).
    The restricted maximized log-likelihood is:
    \[\ell(\hat{\mu}_0, \hat{\sigma}_0^2) = -\frac{n}{2}\ln(2\pi) - \frac{n}{2}\ln(\hat{\sigma}_0^2) - \frac{n}{2}\]
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Define the Test Statistic \(D\):} The test statistic \(D\) is
  defined as twice the difference between the unrestricted and
  restricted log-likelihoods:
  \[D = 2 \left[ \ell(\hat{\mu}, \hat{\sigma}^2) - \ell(\hat{\mu}_0, \hat{\sigma}_0^2) \right]\]
  Substituting the expressions above, the constant terms cancel out:
  \[D = 2 \left[ -\frac{n}{2}\ln(\hat{\sigma}^2) - \left( -\frac{n}{2}\ln(\hat{\sigma}_0^2) \right) \right] = n \ln \left( \frac{\hat{\sigma}_0^2}{\hat{\sigma}^2} \right)\]
\item
  \textbf{Relate to the t-statistic:} Using the sum of squares
  decomposition
  \(\hat{\sigma}_0^2 = \hat{\sigma}^2 + (\bar{X} - \mu_0)^2\), we have:
  \[\frac{\hat{\sigma}_0^2}{\hat{\sigma}^2} = 1 + \frac{(\bar{X} - \mu_0)^2}{\hat{\sigma}^2}\]
  Substituting the t-statistic
  \(T = \frac{\bar{X} - \mu_0}{S/\sqrt{n}}\), where
  \(S^2 = \frac{n}{n-1}\hat{\sigma}^2\), we find:
  \[\frac{(\bar{X} - \mu_0)^2}{\hat{\sigma}^2} = \frac{T^2}{n-1}\] Thus,
  \(D\) is a monotone increasing function of \(T^2\):
  \[D = n \ln \left( 1 + \frac{T^2}{n-1} \right)\] Rejecting \(H_0\) for
  large \(D\) is equivalent to rejecting for large \(|T|\).
\item
  \textbf{Asymptotic Distribution (Wilks' Theorem):} By Wilks' Theorem,
  \(D \xrightarrow{d} \chi^2_1\). For large \(n\), using the
  approximation \(\ln(1+x) \approx x\):
  \[D = n \ln \left( 1 + \frac{T^2}{n-1} \right) \approx n \left( \frac{T^2}{n-1} \right) \approx T^2\]

  As \(n \to \infty\), \(T^2 \xrightarrow{d} \chi^2_1\), confirming the
  theorem.
\end{enumerate}

\end{example}

\section{A Short List of Contributors to Statistical Inference Based On
Likelihood}\label{a-short-list-of-contributors-to-statistical-inference-based-on-likelihood}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{R.A. Fisher (1922, 1925)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Affiliation:} Rothamsted / University College London (UCL)
  \item
    \emph{Connection:} The ``father'' of the field; his work at UCL
    influenced the entire next generation, including Rao and Pearson.
  \item
    \textbf{Contribution:} Fisher formally defined \textbf{Likelihood}
    as a function distinct from probability (\(L(\theta; x)\) vs
    \(f(x; \theta)\)). He introduced the \textbf{Maximum Likelihood
    Estimator (MLE)}, derived the \textbf{Fisher Information} measure,
    and established the asymptotic efficiency of MLEs.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{J. Neyman \& E.S. Pearson (1928, 1933)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Affiliations:} UC Berkeley (Neyman) / UCL (Pearson)
  \item
    \emph{Connection:} Egon Pearson was Karl Pearson's son (Fisher's
    rival). Neyman founded the Berkeley Statistics department, which
    later hosted Lehmann, Scheffé, and Le Cam.
  \item
    \textbf{Contribution:} They introduced the \textbf{Likelihood Ratio}
    criterion (\(\lambda\)) as a general method for hypothesis testing.
    Their 1933 Lemma proved that for simple hypotheses, the Likelihood
    Ratio Test is the \textbf{Uniformly Most Powerful (UMP)} test,
    establishing the optimality of likelihood-based methods.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{S.S. Wilks (1938)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Affiliation:} Princeton University
  \item
    \emph{Connection:} A student of Henry Rietz, Wilks spent time at UCL
    with Pearson and Wishart before establishing the Princeton program.
  \item
    \textbf{Contribution:} Wilks derived the asymptotic distribution of
    the Likelihood Ratio statistic for composite hypotheses. He proved
    that \(-2 \ln \Lambda\) converges to a \textbf{Chi-square
    distribution} with degrees of freedom equal to the difference in the
    number of free parameters.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{M.S. Bartlett (1937)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Affiliation:} UCL / University of Manchester
  \item
    \emph{Connection:} A student of Fisher and colleague of Pearson at
    UCL; famously debated with Fisher on conditional inference.
  \item
    \textbf{Contribution:} Bartlett improved the accuracy of the
    Likelihood Ratio Test for small samples. He introduced the
    \textbf{Bartlett Correction}, a scaling factor for the test
    statistic that aligns its expected value with that of the limiting
    Chi-square distribution.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{A. Wald (1943)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Affiliation:} Columbia University
  \item
    \emph{Connection:} A student of Karl Menger in Vienna; at Columbia,
    he worked with Wolfowitz and influenced the decision-theoretic
    approach adopted by Stein and Karlin.
  \item
    \textbf{Contribution:} Wald developed the \textbf{Wald Test}, which
    tests hypotheses based on the distance between the unrestricted MLE
    and the hypothesized value. He demonstrated the asymptotic
    equivalence of the Likelihood Ratio, Wald, and Score tests for
    standard models.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{H. Cramér (1946)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Affiliation:} Stockholm University
  \item
    \emph{Connection:} Hosted Rao in Stockholm; his textbook synthesized
    the British (Fisher/Pearson) and American (Wilks/Wald) schools into
    one rigorous framework.
  \item
    \textbf{Contribution:} In his influential text \emph{Mathematical
    Methods of Statistics}, Cramér provided rigorous proofs for the
    consistency and asymptotic normality of the MLE. Independently of
    Rao, he established the \textbf{Cramér-Rao Lower Bound}.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{C.R. Rao (1948)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Affiliation:} Indian Statistical Institute (ISI)
  \item
    \emph{Connection:} A PhD student of Fisher at Cambridge; he unified
    the testing theories of Fisher, Neyman, and Wald.
  \item
    \textbf{Contribution:} Rao introduced the \textbf{Score Test} (or
    Lagrange Multiplier Test), which allows for hypothesis testing using
    only the restricted MLE (under the null hypothesis). He also
    independently derived the lower bound for estimator variance.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{E.L. Lehmann \& H. Scheffé (1950, 1955)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Affiliation:} UC Berkeley
  \item
    \emph{Connection:} Lehmann was Neyman's student at Berkeley; Scheffé
    was a colleague. They solidified the ``Berkeley School'' of
    optimality.
  \item
    \textbf{Contribution:} Building on the concept of
    \textbf{Sufficiency}, they established the \textbf{Lehmann-Scheffé
    Theorem}. This result connects complete sufficient statistics to
    \textbf{Uniformly Minimum Variance Unbiased Estimators (UMVUE)}.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{S. Karlin \& H. Rubin (1956)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Affiliation:} Stanford University
  \item
    \emph{Connection:} Part of the ``Stanford School'' (along with
    Stein) that focused on decision theory, heavily influenced by Wald's
    earlier work.
  \item
    \textbf{Contribution:} They formalized the property of
    \textbf{Monotone Likelihood Ratio (MLR)} for families of
    distributions. The \textbf{Karlin-Rubin Theorem} extended the
    Neyman-Pearson Lemma to one-sided composite hypotheses.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{W. James \& C. Stein (1961)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Affiliation:} Stanford University
  \item
    \emph{Connection:} Stein was a student of Wald at Columbia before
    moving to Stanford.
  \item
    \textbf{Contribution:} They discovered \textbf{Stein's Paradox},
    showing that the MLE (sample mean) is \textbf{inadmissible} for
    estimating the mean of a multivariate Normal distribution
    (\(p \ge 3\)). They introduced the \textbf{James-Stein Estimator}, a
    shrinkage estimator that dominates the MLE.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{L. Le Cam (1960s)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Affiliation:} UC Berkeley
  \item
    \emph{Connection:} A student of Neyman at Berkeley; he took the
    asymptotic torch from Wald and Wilks and placed it on a rigorous
    topological foundation.
  \item
    \textbf{Contribution:} Le Cam modernized asymptotic theory by
    introducing \textbf{Local Asymptotic Normality (LAN)}. He showed
    that under general conditions, the likelihood ratio of a complex
    experiment behaves asymptotically like that of a Normal shift
    experiment.
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}




\end{document}
